   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Comparison of Portuguese and Spanish
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This article
          <b>
           possibly contains
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
            original research
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit">
            improve it
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verifying
           </a>
           the claims made and adding
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
            inline citations
           </a>
           . Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (January 2010)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
         Portuguese
        </a>
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
         Spanish
        </a>
       </b>
       , although closely related
       <a href="/wiki/Sister_language" title="Sister language">
        sister languages
       </a>
       , differ in many details of their phonology, grammar, and lexicon. Both are part of a broader group known as
       <a href="/wiki/West_Iberian_languages" title="West Iberian languages">
        West Iberian Romance
       </a>
       , which also includes several other languages or dialects with fewer speakers, all of which are mutually intelligible to some degree.
      </p>
      <p>
       The most obvious differences are in pronunciation. Mutual intelligibility is generally greater for the written languages than for their spoken forms. Compare, for example, the following sentences:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Al buen entendedor pocas palabras bastan
        </i>
        (Spanish:
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [al ˈβ̞we̞n e̞nte̞nde̞ˈð̞o̞r ˈpo̞kas paˈlaβ̞ɾaz ˈβ̞astan]
        </span>
        )
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Ao bom entendedor poucas palavras bastam
        </i>
        (European Portuguese:
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɜw ˈβõ̞(ɰ̃) ẽ̞tẽ̞dʊ̜̆ˈðoɾ ˈpo(w)kə̻̆ɕ pə̆ˈlavɾə̆ʑ ˈβaɕtə̃w]
        </span>
        , Brazilian Portuguese:
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɐw ˈbõ̞(w̃) ĩtẽ̞de̞ˈdox ˈpo(w)kə̻̆s pɐˈlavɾə̆z ˈbaɕtə̃w]
        </span>
        )
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       —roughly equivalent to the English proverb "A word to the wise is sufficient," or, a more literal translation, "To a good listener, a few words are enough."
      </p>
      <p>
       There are also some significant differences between
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
        Brazilian Portuguese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
        European Portuguese
       </a>
       as there are between
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_and_British_English_differences" title="American and British English differences">
        British and American English
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Spanish" title="Peninsular Spanish">
        Peninsular
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_American_Spanish" title="Latin American Spanish">
        Latin American Spanish
       </a>
       . This article notes these differences below only where:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        both Brazilian and European Portuguese differ not only from each other, but from Spanish as well;
       </li>
       <li>
        both Peninsular (i.e. European) and Latin American Spanish differ not only from each other, but also from Portuguese; or
       </li>
       <li>
        either Brazilian or European Portuguese differs from Spanish with
        <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
         syntax
        </a>
        not possible in Spanish (while the other dialect does not).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          <img alt="[]" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="75" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" width="30"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
           IPA
          </a>
          phonetic symbols.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
          Unicode
         </a>
         characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_texts">
        Sample texts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sample texts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Portuguese and Spanish share a great number of words that are spelled either identically or almost identically (although the pronunciation almost always differs), or which differ sometimes in predictable ways. Consider, for example, the following paragraph, taken from the
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         <i>
          Gramática esencial del español
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       , by
       <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Seco" title="Manuel Seco">
        Manuel Seco
       </a>
       (Espasa Calpe, 1989), and compare it to the Portuguese rendition below, noting the lexical similarity and the slight changes in word order:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        <i>
         Pero, a pesar de esta variedad de posibilidades que la voz posee, sería un muy pobre instrumento de comunicación si no contara más que con ella. La capacidad de expresión del hombre no dispondría de más medios que la de los animales. La voz, sola, es para el hombre escasamente una materia informe, que para convertirse en un instrumento perfecto de comunicación debe ser sometida a un cierto tratamiento. Esa manipulación que recibe la voz son las "articulaciones".
        </i>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        <i>
         Mas, apesar da variedade de possibilidades que a voz possui, seria um instrumento de comunicação muito pobre se não se contasse com mais do que ela. A capacidade de expressão do homem não disporia de mais meios que a dos animais. A voz, sozinha, é para o homem apenas uma matéria informe, que para se converter num instrumento perfeito de comunicação deve ser submetida a um certo tratamento. Essa manipulação que a voz recebe são as "articulações".
        </i>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">
        Vocabulary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cognates">
        Cognates
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cognates">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Most of the frequent words in the two languages share a common origin in Latin,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but several of these
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognates" title="Cognates">
        cognates
       </a>
       differ, to a greater or lesser extent, in meaning.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Two_forms_vs._one_form">
        Two forms vs. one form
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Two forms vs. one form">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Some words have two forms in one language, but just one in the other:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Portuguese
        <i>
         criar
        </i>
        corresponds to both Spanish
        <i>
         crear
        </i>
        'to create' and
        <i>
         criar
        </i>
        'to raise'.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         sueño
        </i>
        (which means 'dream' and also is used to say that one is sleepy) corresponds to both Portuguese
        <i>
         sonho
        </i>
        'dream' and
        <i>
         sono
        </i>
        'sleep' (the former from Latin
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         somnium
        </span>
        and the latter from
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         somnus
        </span>
        , which produced the same outcome in Spanish).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="False_friends">
        False friends
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: False friends">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Some pairs of cognates differ in that they have a broader or narrower meaning in one language than in the other, or their meanings are entirely different. On this basis they are termed "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_friends" title="False friends">
        false friends
       </a>
       ":
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          cola
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          cauda
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           fila
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           bicha
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           cauda
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           rabo
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           linha
          </i>
          (&lt; Fr.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           file
          </span>
          , &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           bestius
          </span>
          , &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           cauda
          </span>
          , &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           rapum
          </span>
          , &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           linea
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         queue, file, tail
        </td>
       </tr>

          ,
          <i>
           plaga
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           peste
          </i>
          (&lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           infirmitas
          </span>
          , &lt; Ar.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           saka
          </span>
          , &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           plaga
          </span>
          , &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           pestis
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          disease, plague
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          serviço
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          servitium
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           servicio
          </i>
          (&lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           servitium
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          service
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          perro
         </i>
         (&lt;
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          uncertain
         </span>
         , maybe Sp. perro')
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           oxidado
          </i>
          (&lt; Greek + Lat. suffix
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           oxis + tatus
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          rusty
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          berro
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          barrire
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           chillido
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           berrido
          </i>
          (&lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           cisclare
          </span>
          , &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           barrire
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          screech, shriek
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          aceite
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          acceptāre
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           aceptado
          </i>
          (&lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           acceptāre
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          accepted
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          oficina
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          &lt; officium &lt; officina
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           taller
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           taller de coches
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           taller mecánico de autos
          </i>
          (&lt; Old Fr.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           astelier
          </span>
          , &lt; Old Fr. + Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           estalier + cocca
          </span>
          , &lt; Old Fr. + Lat. + Gr.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           astelier + mechanicus + αὐτο
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          auto repair centre, repair garage, workshop
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          firma
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          firmus
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           empresa; compañía; sociedad, negocio
          </i>
          (&lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           prenhendere, companio, societas, nec otium
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          business, firm, company, corporation, enterprise, venture, establishment, group, house
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          presunto
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          persuctus
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           jamón
          </i>
          (&lt; Fr.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           jambon
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          ham, prosciutto
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          risco
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          reseca
         </span>
         )
        </td>

         <p>
          <i>
           riesgo
          </i>
          (&lt; Arabic
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           rizq
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
            <a href="#cite_note-2">
             [2]
            </a>
           </sup>
           or maybe Italian 'rischio'
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>

         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>

         </i>

         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          tópos
         </span>

          </i>
          (&lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           columen
          </span>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          top
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Semantic_change">
        Semantic change
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Semantic change">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Many pairs of cognates have come to have different meanings due to
       <a href="/wiki/Semantic_change" title="Semantic change">
        semantic change
       </a>
       . These false friends include the following:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         diseñar
        </i>
        means 'to design' in Spanish, while its Portuguese cognate
        <i>
         desenhar
        </i>
        means 'to draw'.
       </li>
       <li>
        Similarly,
        <i>
         dibujo
        </i>
        is Spanish for 'drawing', but
        <i>
         debuxo
        </i>
        means 'sketch' in Portuguese (although it is rather rare and bookish, having been largely displaced by
        <i>
         rascunho
        </i>
        ; cf. Spanish
        <i>
         rasguño
        </i>
        , which means 'scratch').
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         largo
        </i>
        (also archaic
        <i>
         luengo
        </i>
        ) means 'long', while
        <i>
         ancho
        </i>
        means 'wide'. In Portuguese
        <i>
         largo
        </i>
        (also
        <i>
         ancho
        </i>
        ) is 'wide' and
        <i>
         longo
        </i>
        (a cognate of the Sp. archaic "luengo") is 'long'.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         extrañar
        </i>
        can mean 'to find strange' or 'to miss'. Portuguese
        <i>
         estranhar
        </i>
        means 'to find strange', or
        <i>
         to lock horns
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         raro
        </i>
        can mean 'rare' or 'strange'. In Portuguese, it just means 'rare'.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         aún
        </i>
        can mean 'yet/still' and
        <i>
         todavía
        </i>
        can mean both 'yet/still' or 'however/nevertheless'. Portuguese
        <i>
         todavia
        </i>
        means 'however/nevertheless'. In Portuguese, 'yet/still' is
        <i>
         ainda
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         (estar) embarazada
        </i>
        means '(to be) pregnant'. Portuguese
        <i>
         (estar) embaraçada
        </i>
        means '(to be) embarrassed' or '(to be) entangled'.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-3">
          [3]
         </a>
        </sup>
        However, Spanish does have the term
        <i>
         embarazoso/a
        </i>
        meaning 'embarrassing'. 'Pregnant' in Portuguese is
        <i>
         grávida
        </i>
        . The Portuguese
        <i>
         prenha
        </i>
        and Spanish
        <i>
         preñada
        </i>
        are used mainly for pregnant animals but rarely for women, in both languages
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         exquisito
        </i>
        means 'exquisite/sophisticated'. Portuguese
        <i>
         esquisito
        </i>
        means 'strange/weird'.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Experto
        </i>
        means 'expert' in both Spanish and Portuguese, but in Portuguese it should not be confused with its
        <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">
         homophone
        </a>
        <i>
         esperto
        </i>
        (a homophone only in
        <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
         Brazil
        </a>
        ), which means 'smart/intelligent'. 'Expert' in Portuguese may also be
        <i>
         perito
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         especialista
        </i>
        , or
        <i>
         exímio
        </i>
        , which are the same in Spanish (Sp.
        <i>
         eximio
        </i>
        is spelled without the accent mark).
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         escoba
        </i>
        is 'broom'. Portuguese
        <i>
         escova
        </i>
        is 'brush' or 'broom' (but Portuguese usually chooses 'vassoura' for 'broom'). However, in some varieties of Spanish,
        <i>
         escobilla
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         escobeta
        </i>
        means 'toilet brush'.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         apellido
        </i>
        '
        <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">
         surname
        </a>
        ' is
        <i>
         apelido
        </i>
        in European Portuguese, and
        <i>
         sobrenome
        </i>
        in both Brazilian and European Portuguese (but Portuguese usually say
        <i>
         apelido
        </i>
        ). Spanish
        <i>
         sobrenombre
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         apodo
        </i>
        '
        <a href="/wiki/Nickname" title="Nickname">
         nickname
        </a>
        ' is
        <i>
         apelido/alcunha/codinome
        </i>
        in Brazilian Portuguese, and
        <i>
         alcunha
        </i>
        in European Portuguese.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         rojo
        </i>
        is 'red'. Portuguese
        <i>
         roxo
        </i>
        is 'purple'. 'Red' in Portuguese is
        <i>
         vermelho
        </i>
        . In European Portuguese the word
        <i>
         encarnado
        </i>
        (literally
        <i>
         in the flesh
        </i>
        ) is also used as synonym of 'red' even though
        <i>
         vermelho
        </i>
        is more frequent.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         apenas
        </i>
        means 'hardly'. Portuguese
        <i>
         apenas
        </i>
        is 'only'. Thus the Spanish phrase
        <i>
         él apenas pudo dormir
        </i>
        means 'he could not even/hardly sleep', or 'he was just barely able to sleep', whereas the Portuguese phrase
        <i>
         ele pôde apenas dormir
        </i>
        means 'he could only sleep'.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         vaso
        </i>
        means 'drinking glass (tumbler)', while Portuguese
        <i>
         vaso
        </i>
        means
        <i>
         toilet
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         vaso sanitário
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         váter
        </i>
        in Spanish) or
        <i>
         flower pot
        </i>
        . A 'drinking glass' in Portuguese is
        <i>
         copo
        </i>
        , while Spanish
        <i>
         copa
        </i>
        is a wine glass. A wine glass in Portuguese is
        <i>
         copo
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         taça
        </i>
        , while Spanish
        <i>
         taza
        </i>
        is a 'coffee cup or teacup'; Spanish
        <i>
         taza
        </i>
        also refers to the bathroom bowl. 'Coffee cup' in Portuguese is
        <i>
         xícara de café
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         chávena
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish
        <i>
         cachorro
        </i>
        means
        <a href="/wiki/Puppy" title="Puppy">
         puppy
        </a>
        , while in Portuguese, it can refer to a dog of any age.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Frequent_function_words">
        Frequent function words
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Frequent function words">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       A number of the frequent "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Function_words" title="Function words">
        function words
       </a>
       " (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronouns" title="Pronouns">
        pronouns
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Conjunctions" title="Conjunctions">
        conjunctions
       </a>
       , etc.) are cognates in the two languages but are used in slightly different ways, including the following:
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span id="Spanish_todo.2C_Portuguese_tudo">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_todo,_Portuguese_tudo">
        Spanish
        <i>
         todo
        </i>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         tudo
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Spanish todo, Portuguese tudo">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       The Spanish pronoun
       <i>
        todo
       </i>
       can mean 'all/every', or 'everything'. Portuguese distinguishes between
       <i>
        todo
       </i>
       'all/every' (masculine) and
       <i>
        tudo
       </i>
       'everything' (neuter, used for an indefinite object or abstraction).
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Todos
         </b>
         los insectos tienen seis patas.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Todos
         </b>
         os insetos têm seis patas.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         All
        </b>
        insects have six legs.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         El ladrón lo robó
         <b>
          todo
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         O ladrão roubou
         <b>
          tudo
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'The thief stole
        <b>
         everything'
        </b>
        or 'The thief stole
        <b>
         it all
        </b>
        .'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Relative_and_interrogative_pronouns">
        Relative and interrogative pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Relative and interrogative pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Spanish uses an
       <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
        acute accent
       </a>
       on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interrogative_pronoun" title="Interrogative pronoun">
        interrogative pronouns
       </a>
       , while the corresponding
       <a href="/wiki/Relative_pronoun" title="Relative pronoun">
        relative pronouns
       </a>
       (etymologically the same words) are spelled without the accent to mark the difference in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosodic_stress" title="Prosodic stress">
        prosodic stress
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (As explained below, the acute accent often changes the vowel sound in Portuguese, but not Spanish.) For example,
       <i>
        ¿quién?
       </i>
       (who?) and
       <i>
        quien
       </i>
       (who) in Spanish, but
       <i>
        quem
       </i>
       for both in Portuguese. Apart from that, while
       <i>
        "quem"
       </i>
       is invariable, Spanish has both the singular
       <i>
        "quién"
       </i>
       and the plural
       <i>
        "quiénes."
       </i>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        ¿Quién
        <b>
         es
        </b>
        son esas personas? (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Quem são essas pessoas?
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Who are those people?'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In the Portuguese example, the "m" at the end of "quem" is never pronounced. It is merely a marker for nasalization.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span id="Spanish_muy_and_mucho.2C_Portuguese_muito">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_muy_and_mucho,_Portuguese_muito">
        Spanish
        <i>
         muy
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         mucho
        </i>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         muito
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Spanish muy and mucho, Portuguese muito">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Spanish distinguishes the adjective
       <i>
        mucho
       </i>
       'much/many' from the adverb
       <i>
        muy
       </i>
       'very/quite'. Portuguese uses
       <i>
        muito
       </i>
       for both (there's also
       <i>
        mui
       </i>
       , but it is considered old-fashioned)
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Saqué
         <b>
          muchas
         </b>
         fotos durante el viaje.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Tirei
         <b>
          muitas
         </b>
         fotos durante a viagem.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'I took
        <b>
         many
        </b>
        photos during the trip.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Las cerezas están
         <b>
          muy
         </b>
         maduras.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         As cerejas estão
         <b>
          muito
         </b>
         maduras.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'The cherries are
        <b>
         quite
        </b>
        ripe.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       As an adjective,
       <i>
        muito
       </i>
       is
       <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">
        inflected
       </a>
       according to the gender and number of the noun it qualifies, like
       <i>
        mucho
       </i>
       . As an adverb, it is invariable like
       <i>
        muy
       </i>
       . Thus, it would be incorrect to say *
       <i>
        muitas maduras
       </i>
       in the second example.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cardinal_numbers">
        Cardinal numbers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cardinal numbers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       The cardinal numbers are very similar in Spanish and Portuguese, but there are differences of usage in numbers one and two. Spanish has different words for the masculine singular indefinite article ('a, an') and the numeral 'one', thus
       <i>
        un capítulo
       </i>
       'a chapter', but
       <i>
        capítulo uno
       </i>
       'chapter one'. In Portuguese, both words are the same:
       <i>
        um capítulo
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        capítulo um
       </i>
       . Spanish
       <i>
        uno
       </i>
       can also be used as a pronoun, like the English
       <a href="/wiki/One_(pronoun)" title="One (pronoun)">
        generic "one"
       </a>
       , to represent an indeterminate subject, but this is not possible with Portuguese
       <i>
        um
       </i>
       ; the reflexive pronoun
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       is used instead.
       <i>
        Se
       </i>
       may be used in Spanish to form passive and impersonal constructions, as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Uno
         </b>
         (
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         <b>
          Se
         </b>
         ) debe pensar antes de actuar.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Deve-
         <b>
          se
         </b>
         pensar antes de agir.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         One
        </b>
        should think before acting.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       This still applies in cases where a relatively indeterminate subject is genderized, such as the Spanish
       <i>
        todos a una [voz]
       </i>
       ('all at once', literally 'all at one [voice]'). It should be rewritten in Portuguese without any cardinal number. For example,
       <i>
        todos juntos
       </i>
       'all together'.
      </p>
      <p>
       On the other hand, in Portuguese, cardinal number 'two' inflects with gender (
       <i>
        dois
       </i>
       if masculine,
       <i>
        duas
       </i>
       if feminine), while in Spanish
       <i>
        dos
       </i>
       is used for both.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Uno más uno es igual a
         <b>
          dos
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Um mais um é igual a
         <b>
          dois
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'One plus one equals
        <b>
         two
        </b>
        .'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Dos
         </b>
         cabezas piensan mejor que una.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Duas
         </b>
         cabeças pensam melhor que uma.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         Two
        </b>
        heads think better than one.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Tengo
         <b>
          dos
         </b>
         hermanos y
         <b>
          dos
         </b>
         hermanas.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Tenho
         <b>
          dois
         </b>
         irmãos e
         <b>
          duas
         </b>
         irmãs.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'I have
        <b>
         two
        </b>
        brothers and
        <b>
         two
        </b>
        sisters.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Conjunctions">
        Conjunctions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conjunctions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       The conjunction "and" in Spanish is
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       (pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       before a consonant,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       before a vowel) before all words except those beginning with an
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       sound (spelled
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       - or
       <i>
        hi
       </i>
       -). Before a syllabic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       sound (and
       <i>
        <b>
         not
        </b>
       </i>
       the diphthong
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [je]
       </span>
       as in
       <i>
        hierro
       </i>
       ), the Spanish conjunction is
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [e̞]
       </span>
       . Portuguese uses
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       before all words.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Sal
         <b>
          y
         </b>
         pimienta.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Sal
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         pimenta.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Salt
        <b>
         and
        </b>
        pepper.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Judío
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         hindú.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Judeu
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         hindu.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Jewish
        <b>
         and
        </b>
        Hindu.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Leones
         <b>
          y
         </b>
         hienas.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Leões
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         hienas.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Lions
        <b>
         and
        </b>
        hyenas.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Similarly, for the conjunction "or" Spanish uses
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o̞]
       </span>
       before all words except those beginning with
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       - or
       <i>
        ho
       </i>
       -, in which case it uses
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [w]
       </span>
       . Portuguese always uses
       <i>
        ou
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ow]
       </span>
       ~
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Vino
         <b>
          o
         </b>
         agua.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Vinho
         <b>
          ou
         </b>
         água.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Wine
        <b>
         or
        </b>
        water.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Uno
         <b>
          u
         </b>
         otro.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Um
         <b>
          ou
         </b>
         outro.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'One
        <b>
         or
        </b>
        the other.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h5>
       <span id="Se.2C_si.2C_s.C3.AD.2C_and_sim">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Se,_si,_sí,_and_sim">
        <i>
         Se
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         si
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         sí
        </i>
        , and
        <i>
         sim
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Se, si, sí, and sim">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In Portuguese, the word
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       can be a reflexive pronoun or a conjunction meaning 'if'. This may give the
       <i>
        <b>
         false
        </b>
       </i>
       impression that a Portuguese verb
       <i>
        <b>
         is
        </b>
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_verb" title="Reflexive verb">
        pronominal
       </a>
       when it is
       <i>
        <b>
         not
        </b>
       </i>
       . For example,
       <i>
        Se ficou em Paris...
       </i>
       means 'If one stayed in Paris...' When the conjunction
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       precedes a pronominal verb, it is common to have a double
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       in the sentence, such as
       <i>
        Se se esqueceu da sua senha...
       </i>
       'If you forgot your password...'
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Meaning and description
        </th>
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         'yes'
        </td>
        <td rowspan="2" style="text-align:center;">
         <i>
          sí
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         <i>
          sim
         </i>
         [sĩ ]
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         'himself' / 'herself' / 'itself' / 'themselves'
         <br/>
         (stressed
         <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_pronoun" title="Reflexive pronoun">
          reflexive pronoun
         </a>
         , object of preposition)
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         <i>
          si
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         'if' (conjunction)
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         <i>
          si
         </i>
        </td>
        <td rowspan="2" style="text-align:center;">
         <i>
          se
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         /'oneself" /'yourself" / 'itself' / 'themselves'
         <br/>
         (unstressed
         <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_pronoun" title="Reflexive pronoun">
          reflexive pronoun
         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         <i>
          se
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_object_pronouns">
        Indirect object pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Indirect object pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Spanish
       <i>
        le
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        les
       </i>
       are changed to
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       when followed by
       <i>
        lo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        la
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        los
       </i>
       , or
       <i>
        las
       </i>
       . For example, "Mi abuelo
       <b>
        les
       </b>
       compró los regalos" becomes "Mi abuelo
       <b>
        se
       </b>
       los compró". See also
       <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish#Combining_pronouns_in_Spanish" title="Comparison of Portuguese and Spanish">
        "Combining pronouns in Spanish"
       </a>
       below. In addition, Spanish uses
       <i>
        sé
       </i>
       as an irregular verb in the first person singular indicative of
       <i>
        saber
       </i>
       (to know), and the second person singular imperative of
       <i>
        ser
       </i>
       (to be). In Portuguese, these are
       <i>
        sei
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        sê
       </i>
       respectively.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dissimilar_words">
        Dissimilar words
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dissimilar words">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Despite the mostly cognate vocabulary between Spanish and Portuguese, a significant number of common words are entirely different in the two languages (although in some cases cognates exist, but are rare or archaic in one of the two languages). Examples include the following:
      </p>

      <p>
       Vocabulary differences between the two languages arose from various factors:
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="French_influence">
        French influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: French influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Both Portuguese and Spanish have borrowed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loanwords" title="Loanwords">
        loanwords
       </a>
       either directly from
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       or by way of French as an intermediary from other (mostly Greco-Latin) sources. Although no statistical study based on uniform criteria has been carried out, it has been suggested that words of French origin in Portuguese are significantly more numerous than those in Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Here are some examples:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic_influence">
        Arabic influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Arabic influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Spanish kept much of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mozarabic_language" title="Mozarabic language">
        Mozarabic
       </a>
       vocabulary of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       origin, while the Mozarabic component was less influential in Portuguese, even if still found. Thus we find a number of cases in which the usual Spanish word is derived from Arabic, while the corresponding word in Portuguese is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
        Celtic
       </a>
       -derived, as in the following examples:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <th>
         Spanish &lt; Arabic
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         stonemason
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          pedreiro
         </i>
         &lt;
         <i>
          pedra
         </i>
         &lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          petra
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          albañil
         </i>
         &lt;
         <i>
          al-bannī
         </i>
        </td>

         <i>

         </i>
         (Brazil):
         <p>
          &lt;
          <i>
           presidir
          </i>
          &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           praesidēre
          </span>
          ; &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           camera
          </span>
          ; &lt; Lat.
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           praefectus
          </span>
         </p>

      <p>
       There are some examples where a word of Arabic origin is used in Portuguese but not in Spanish, such as: Sp.
       <i>
        romero
       </i>
       , Port.
       <i>
        alecrim
       </i>
       (Port.
       <i>
        rosmaninho
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        rosmarinho
       </i>
       means 'lavender'), 'rosemary'; Sp.
       <i>
        lechuga
       </i>
       , Port.
       <i>
        alface
       </i>
       (in Port.
       <i>
        leituga
       </i>
       means 'catsear'), 'lettuce', or more commonly used in Portuguese than in Spanish although the word exists in both languages, such as:
       <i>
        chafariz
       </i>
       'fountain' (Port.
       <i>
        fonte
       </i>
       , Sp.
       <i>
        fuente
       </i>
       ) or
       <i>
        garrafa
       </i>
       'bottle' (Port.
       <i>
        botelha
       </i>
       , Sp.
       <i>
        botella
       </i>
       ). In a few cases Spanish and Portuguese have both borrowed different Arabic-derived words for the same meaning, such as: Sp.
       <i>
        alfombra
       </i>
       , Port.
       <i>
        alcatifa
       </i>
       , 'carpet'.
      </p>
      <p>
       Arabic is the source of a few personal given names and numerous surnames and place names in Spain, including the following:
      </p>
      <p>
       Almudena,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Azucena,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Carmen,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">

        </a>
       </sup>
       Guadalupe,

       Mohamed,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">

       Soraya,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">

       Abenamir, Abengoa, Avengoa, Abenójar, Alcalá,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">

       </sup>
       Almuzara, Acebrón, Aceituno, Aceitón, Aguera,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">

       Aguiló, Alamar, Alamino, Alanzor, Albarral, Albarrán, Albo, Alcantud, Alcazar,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">

       Alcudia, Alguacil, Allobar, Almaguer, Almandós, Almandoz, Almodóvar, Almoravit, Ambasil, Amor, Andujar, Aranda, Ayas, Aias, Benayas, Bardaxí, Benajara, Benameji,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
