      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Influences_from_other_languages">
        Influences from other languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Influences from other languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Spanish and Portuguese have acquired different words from various
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">
        Amerindian
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_languages" title="African languages">
        African
       </a>
       and Asian languages, as in the following examples:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <ul>
           <li>
            '
            <a href="/wiki/Pineapple#The_name" title="Pineapple">
             pineapple
            </a>
            ': Sp.
            <i>
             piña
            </i>
            (from the Spanish word for 'pine cone') / Port.
            <i>
             abacaxi
            </i>
            (from
            <a href="/wiki/Tupian_languages" title="Tupian languages">
             Tupi
            </a>
            ) or
            <i>
             ananás
            </i>
            (from
            <a href="/wiki/Tupi%E2%80%93Guarani_languages" title="Tupi–Guarani languages">
             Tupi–Guarani
            </a>
            ; also in Spanish, by way of Portuguese,
            <i>
             ananás
            </i>
            or
            <i>
             ananá
            </i>
            ).
           </li>
           <li>
            '
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoking_pipe_(tobacco)" title="Smoking pipe (tobacco)">
             smoking pipe
            </a>
            ': Sp.
            <i>
             pipa
            </i>
            (from supposed Late Latin
            <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
             pīpa
            </span>
            ) / Port.
            <i>
             cachimbo
            </i>
            (from
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kimbundu_language" title="Kimbundu language">
             Kimbundu
            </a>
            ).
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
             <a href="#cite_note-52">
              [52]
             </a>
            </sup>
           </li>
           <li>
            '
            <a href="/wiki/Tea#Etymology_and_cognates_in_other_languages" title="Tea">
             tea
            </a>
            ': Sp.
            <i>
             té
            </i>
            (from
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Min_Nan" title="Min Nan">
             Min Nan
            </a>
            Chinese) / Port.
            <i>
             chá
            </i>
            (from
            <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">
             Cantonese
            </a>
            ).
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
             <a href="#cite_note-53">
              [53]
             </a>
            </sup>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Days_of_the_week">
        Days of the week
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Days of the week">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Unlike the other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       , modern Portuguese does not use the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
        Roman
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_days_of_the_week" title="Names of the days of the week">
        planetary system
       </a>
       for the days Monday through Friday. Instead, the weekdays are numerical, and derived from
       <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_Latin" title="Ecclesiastical Latin">
        Ecclesiastical Latin
       </a>
       . The word
       <i>
        feira
       </i>
       (from Latin
       <a href="/wiki/Feria" title="Feria">
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         fēria
        </span>
       </a>
       ) refers to daily (Roman Catholic) religious celebrations; it is cognate with
       <a class="extiw" href="https://pt.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feira" title="pt:Feira">
        <i>
         feira
        </i>
       </a>
       'fair' or 'market', as well as with
       <i>
        férias
       </i>
       'vacation' and
       <i>
        feriado
       </i>
       'holiday'. In Spanish, the days of the week are all masculine; in Portuguese, the
       <i>
        feira
       </i>
       days are feminine, while
       <i>
        sábado
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        domingo
       </i>
       are masculine.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          lunes
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          diēs lūnae
         </span>
         'Moon's day')
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           segunda-feira
          </i>
          ('second
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           fēria
          </span>
          ')
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         Monday
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          martes
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          diēs martis
         </span>
         'Mars' day')
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           terça-feira
          </i>
          ('third
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           fēria
          </span>
          ')
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Tuesday
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          miércoles
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          diēs mercuriī
         </span>
         'Mercury's day')
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           quarta-feira
          </i>
          ('fourth
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           fēria
          </span>
          ')
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Wednesday
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          jueves
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          diēs iovis
         </span>
         'Jupiter's day')
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           quinta-feira
          </i>
          ('fifth
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           fēria
          </span>
          ')
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Thursday
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <i>
          viernes
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          diēs veneris
         </span>
         , 'Venus' day')
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           sexta-feira
          </i>
          ('sixth
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           fēria
          </span>
          ')
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Friday
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;">
         <i>
          sábado
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          sabbatum
         </span>
         'Sabbath')
        </td>
        <td>
         Saturday
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;">
         <i>
          domingo
         </i>
         (&lt; Lat.
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          diēs dominica
         </span>
         'Lord's day')
        </td>
        <td>
         Sunday
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The form
       <i>
        terça-feira
       </i>
       (&lt; Lat.
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        tertia fēria
       </span>
       ) differs in its first component from the usual Portuguese word for 'third',
       <i>
        terceira
       </i>
       (&lt; Lat.
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        tertiāria
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       In actual usage, the word
       <i>
        feira
       </i>
       is often dropped:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Vou visitar-te na segunda.
        </i>
        (European Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Vou te visitar na segunda.
        </i>
        (Brazilian and European Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'I'll visit you on Monday.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Broadly speaking, the grammars of Portuguese and Spanish share many common features. Nevertheless, some differences between them can present hurdles to people acquainted with one and learning the other.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">
        Gender
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gender">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Spanish has three forms for the singular
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">
        definite article
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        el
       </i>
       , masculine,
       <i>
        la
       </i>
       , feminine, and
       <i>
        lo
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
        neuter
       </a>
       . The last is used with adjectives to form abstract nouns employed in a generic sense, and also to intensify the meaning of adjectives. In Portuguese, there is only
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       , masculine, and
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       , feminine. Literary Spanish has also three corresponding third person pronouns,
       <i>
        él
       </i>
       'he',
       <i>
        ella
       </i>
       'she', and
       <i>
        ello
       </i>
       'it' (referring to a broad concept, not a named object), while Portuguese has only
       <i>
        ele
       </i>
       , masculine, and
       <i>
        ela
       </i>
       , feminine. The Spanish neuters
       <i>
        lo
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ello
       </i>
       have no plural forms.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some words are masculine in Spanish, but feminine in Portuguese, or vice versa. A common example are nouns ended in
       <i>
        -aje
       </i>
       in Spanish, which are masculine, and their Portuguese cognates ending in
       <i>
        -agem
       </i>
       , which are feminine. For example, Spanish
       <i>
        el viaje
       </i>
       'the journey' (masculine, like French
       <i>
        le voyage
       </i>
       and Italian
       <i>
        il viaggio
       </i>
       ) corresponds to the Portuguese feminine
       <i>
        a viagem
       </i>
       . Similarly,
       <i>
        el puente
       </i>
       'bridge',
       <i>
        el dolor
       </i>
       'pain', or
       <i>
        el árbol
       </i>
       'tree' are masculine nouns in Modern Spanish, whereas
       <i>
        a ponte
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        a dor
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        a árvore
       </i>
       are feminine in Portuguese. On the other hand, the Spanish feminine
       <i>
        la leche
       </i>
       'the milk' corresponds to Portuguese
       <i>
        o leite
       </i>
       (masculine, like French
       <i>
        le lait
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        il latte
       </i>
       ). Likewise,
       <i>
        nariz
       </i>
       'nose' is feminine in Spanish and masculine in Portuguese.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some Spanish words can be both masculine and feminine, with different meanings. Both meanings usually exist also in Portuguese, but with one and the same gender, so that they can't be differentiated unless further information is provided. For instance, the word
       <i>
        orden
       </i>
       'order' can mean both 'harmonious arrangement' and 'directive', like its counterparts in English and Portuguese. But the Spanish word is masculine when used with the first meaning, and feminine with the second:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Me sorprendió
         <b>
          el
         </b>
         orden.
        </i>
        ('I was surprised by
        <b>
         the
        </b>
        order [i.e., by how orderly it all was].')
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Me sorprendió
         <b>
          la
         </b>
         orden.
        </i>
        ('I was surprised by
        <b>
         the
        </b>
        order [i.e., by the directive that was given].')
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In Portuguese, the equivalent word
       <i>
        ordem
       </i>
       is always feminine:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Surpreendeu-me
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         ordem.
        </i>
        ('I was surprised by
        <b>
         the
        </b>
        order.')
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Without additional context, it is impossible to tell which meaning was intended in Portuguese and English (though other words could be substituted; in English, one would likely use
       <i>
        orderliness
       </i>
       in the first case above rather than
       <i>
        order
       </i>
       , which would, by itself, suggest the second case).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_the_definite_article">
        Use of the definite article
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Use of the definite article">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In many varieties of Portuguese,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_names" title="Personal names">
        personal names
       </a>
       are normally preceded by a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">
        definite article
       </a>
       , a trait also found in
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       but only in certain dialects in Spanish. In Portuguese, this is a relatively recent development, which some Brazilian dialects have not adopted yet, most notably in some states of the Brazilian Northeast. In those dialects of Portuguese that do regularly use definite articles before proper nouns, the article may be omitted for extra formality, or to show distance in a literary narrative. Compare, for example, English "Mary left", Spanish
       <i>
        María salió
       </i>
       , and Portuguese
       <i>
        A Maria saiu
       </i>
       . Note, however, that in many Spanish dialects the definite article is used before personal names; thus,
       <i>
        la María salió
       </i>
       is commonly heard.
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese uses the definite article before the names of some cities and almost all countries except relatively new ones, such as
       <i>
        Cingapura
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        Singapura
       </i>
       ('Singapore'), and those related to Portugal (or with which Portugal has historical relationships, even though this is a rough rule) and the Portuguese-speaking countries, e.g.,
       <i>
        a Holanda
       </i>
       but
       <i>
        Portugal
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        o México
       </i>
       but
       <i>
        Angola
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        a Suécia
       </i>
       , but
       <i>
        Moçambique
       </i>
       . The major exception to the country rule is
       <i>
        o Brasil
       </i>
       . In Spanish, use of the definite article is optional with some countries:
       <i>
        (la) China
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Japón
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (la) India
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (la) Argentina
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Ecuador
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Perú
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Uruguay
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Paraguay
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Brasil
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (los) Estados Unidos
       </i>
       , etc. The same is true with two continents:
       <i>
        (la) Antártida
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        (el) África
       </i>
       ; with archipelagos and islands:
       <i>
        (las) Filipinas
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (las) Canarias
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (las) Azores
       </i>
       , with some provinces, regions or territories:
       <i>
        (el) Tíbet
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (la) Toscana
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Piamonte
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el) Lacio
       </i>
       and with some cities:
       <i>
        (el) Cairo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (la) Valeta
       </i>
       . Spanish uses the definite article with all geographical names when they appear with an adjective or modifying phrase, as in the following examples:
       <i>
        la España medieval
       </i>
       'medieval Spain',
       <i>
        el Puerto Rico prehispánico
       </i>
       'pre-Hispanic Puerto Rico',
       <i>
        el Portugal de Salazar
       </i>
       'Portugal during Salazar's dictatorship', etc.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Santiago es la capital de Chile.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Santiago é a capital d
         <b>
          o
         </b>
         Chile.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Santiago is the capital of Chile.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Él es de Costa Rica, que está en América Central.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Ele é d
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         Costa Rica, que fica n
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         América Central.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'He is from Costa Rica, which is in Central America.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Tengo un boleto para (
         <b>
          los
         </b>
         ) Estados Unidos de América.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Tenho um bilhete para
         <b>
          os
         </b>
         Estados Unidos d
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         América.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'I have a ticket to
        <b>
         the
        </b>
        United States of America.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Nueva Delhi no es la ciudad más poblada de (
         <b>
          la
         </b>
         ) India
        </i>
        . (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Nova Déli não é a cidade mais populosa d
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         Índia
        </i>
        . (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'New Delhi is not the most populous city in India.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          La
         </b>
         Europa medieval pertenecía a monarcas absolutos
        </i>
        . (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          A
         </b>
         Europa medieval pertencia a monarcas absolutos
        </i>
        . (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Medieval Europe belonged to absolute monarchs.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Portuguese omits the definite article in stating the time of day unless
       <i>
        para as
       </i>
       is used.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Son
         <b>
          las
         </b>
         nueve y cuarto
        </i>
        , but also
        <i>
         Son nueve y quince
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         Son nueve quince
        </i>
        . (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         São (as) nove (horas) e quinze (minutos)
        </i>
        . (Portuguese) (parenthesical parts often omitted)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'It's nine fifteen.' Or:'It's a quarter past/after nine.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In addition, in most dialects of Portuguese the definite article is used before
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_adjective" title="Possessive adjective">
        possessive adjectives
       </a>
       (as it is used in
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       ), which is not possible in Spanish. For instance, the sentence 'This is my brother' is
       <i>
        Este es mi hermano
       </i>
       in Spanish, but may be
       <i>
        Este é
       </i>
       <b>
        o
       </b>
       <i>
        meu irmão
       </i>
       in Portuguese. Nevertheless, in many Brazilian dialects (mostly in the Northeast) and in casual Brazilian Portuguese the article is not used in sentences such as:
       <i>
        Este é meu irmão
       </i>
       (although it usually reappears in sentences such as "
       <i>
        <b>
         O
        </b>
       </i>
       meu irmão está lá").
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Possessives">
        Possessives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Possessives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In Portuguese,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_adjective" title="Possessive adjective">
        possessive adjectives
       </a>
       have the same form as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_pronoun" title="Possessive pronoun">
        possessive pronouns
       </a>
       , and they all agree with the gender of the possessed item. In Spanish, the same is true of
       <i>
        nuestro
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        nuestra
       </i>
       ("our") and
       <i>
        vuestro
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        vuestra
       </i>
       ("your" [plural]), but for all other possessives, the pronoun has a longer form that agrees with the gender of the possessed item, while the adjective has a shorter form that does not change for gender. The possessive adjectives are normally preceded by a definite article in Continental Portuguese, less so in Brazilian Portuguese, and never in Spanish. The possessive pronouns are preceded by a definite article in all dialects of both languages. See examples in the table below.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Gender of
         <br/>
         possessed item
        </th>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
         Portuguese
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Adjective
        </th>
        <th>
         Pronoun
        </th>
        <th>
         Adjective
        </th>
        <th>
         Pronoun
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Feminine
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <b>
           su
          </b>
          casa
         </i>
         <br/>
         "your house"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          la
          <b>
           suya
          </b>
         </i>
         <br/>
         "yours"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          (a)
          <b>
           sua
          </b>
          casa
         </i>
         <br/>
         "your house"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          a
          <b>
           sua
          </b>
         </i>
         <br/>
         "yours"
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Masculine
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <b>
           su
          </b>
          libro
         </i>
         <br/>
         "your book"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          el
          <b>
           suyo
          </b>
         </i>
         <br/>
         "yours"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          (o)
          <b>
           seu
          </b>
          livro
         </i>
         <br/>
         "your book"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          o
          <b>
           seu
          </b>
         </i>
         <br/>
         "yours"
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">
        Pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Object_pronouns">
        Object pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Object pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Portuguese, third-person
       <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">
        clitic
       </a>
       pronouns have special variants used after certain types of verb endings, which does not happen in Spanish. The default object pronouns
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        os
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        as
       </i>
       change to
       <i>
        lo
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        la
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        los
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        las
       </i>
       when they follow a verb that ends in ⟨r⟩, ⟨s⟩ or ⟨z⟩, and to
       <i>
        no
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        na
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        nos
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        nas
       </i>
       when they follow a verb that ends in a nasal sound.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          manténga
          <b>
           lo
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          mantenha-
          <b>
           o
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         'keep it'
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          mantener
          <b>
           lo
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          mantê-
          <b>
           lo
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         'to keep it'
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          <b>
           lo
          </b>
          mantienen
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          mantêm-
          <b>
           no
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         'they keep it'
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In Brazilian Portuguese, these forms are uncommon, since the pronoun normally precedes the verb (i.e.,
       <i>
        você
        <b>
         o
        </b>
        mantenha
       </i>
       in the above example), and third-person subject pronouns are used informally as object pronouns (
       <i>
        mantenha
        <b>
         ele
        </b>
       </i>
       ), which has been proved to be present in the language since Galician-Portuguese times. However, as it has been considered ungrammatical to begin a sentence with an object pronoun, the above examples are, on rare occasion, used in Brazil as well.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Clitic_personal_pronouns">
        Clitic personal pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Clitic personal pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       European Portuguese differs from Brazilian Portuguese with regard to the placement of
       <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">
        clitic
       </a>
       personal pronouns, and Spanish is in turn different from both of them.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        In Spanish, clitic pronouns normally come before the verb, except with the imperative, the infinitive, and the gerund. In verbal periphrases, they precede the
        <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
         auxiliary verb
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        In spoken Brazilian Portuguese, clitic pronouns normally come before the
        <i>
         main
        </i>
        verb. In verbal periphrases, they come between the auxiliary verb and the main verb. This occurs even with the imperative, the infinitive, the gerund, and the past participle.
       </li>
       <li>
        In European Portuguese, clitic pronouns may come before or after the verb, depending on the type of
        <a href="/wiki/Clause" title="Clause">
         clause
        </a>
        . In verbal periphrases, they may precede or follow the auxiliary verb, or follow the main verb (when this is in the infinitive or the gerund).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          Ella
          <b>
           le
          </b>
          dio un libro.
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          Ela deu-
          <b>
           lhe
          </b>
          um livro.
         </i>
         <br/>
         <i>
          Ela
          <b>
           lhe
          </b>
          deu um livro.
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         'She gave
         <b>
          him/her
         </b>
         a book.'
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="left">
         <i>

          <b>

         </i>

         </i>
        </td>

         <b>

         .'
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

          Quería ver
          <b>

         .
         <br/>

          </b>
          quería ver.
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <i>

          <b>
           -te
          </b>
         </i>
         .

          <b>

         </i>

         <b>
          you
         </b>
         .'
        </td>

       <tr>

          <b>

          </b>

         </i>
         <br/>
         <i>

          <b>

          </b>

         </i>

          <b>

          </b>
          .
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <i>
          Não consegui ver-
          <b>
           te
          </b>
          .
         </i>
         <br/>
         <i>
          Não consegui
          <b>
           te
          </b>
          ver.
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         'I didn't manage to see
         <b>
          you
         </b>
         .'
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoclisis">
        Mesoclisis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mesoclisis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Portuguese, verbs in the future indicative or conditional tense may be split into
       <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">
        morphemes
       </a>
       , and the clitic pronoun can be inserted between them, a feature known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoclisis" title="Mesoclisis">
        mesoclisis
       </a>
       . This also occurred in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Spanish" title="Old Spanish">
        Old Spanish
       </a>
       , but no comparable phenomenon takes place in modern Spanish:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Lo
         </b>
         traerá.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Trá-
         <b>
          lo
         </b>
         -á.
        </i>
        (European Portuguese and formal written Brazilian Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'He/She will bring
        <b>
         it
        </b>
        .'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       However, these tenses are often replaced with others in the spoken language. Future indicative is sometimes replaced by present indicative; conditional is very often replaced by imperfect indicative. In colloquial language, most Portuguese would state
       <i>
        trá-lo-á
       </i>
       as
       <i>
        vai trazê-lo
       </i>
       ('going to bring it') or
       <i>
        irá trazê-lo
       </i>
       ('will bring it'). In Brazilian Portuguese, "vai trazer ele" would be the vernacular use.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Combining_pronouns_in_Spanish">
        Combining pronouns in Spanish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Combining pronouns in Spanish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Spanish construction,
       <i>
        se lo dio
       </i>
       , means either '[He/She] gave it to [him/her]' or '[He/She] gave it to
       <i>
        himself/herself'
       </i>
       . The expected pattern for the former would be *
       <i>
        <b>
         le
        </b>
        lo dio
       </i>
       , but such a construction does not exist. This is unique to Spanish.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Latin:
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         dedit illī illud
        </span>
        →
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         dedit illī illum
        </span>
        (early
        <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
         Vulgar Latin
        </a>
        ) →
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         dit illi illu
        </span>
        (Late Vulgar Latin)
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish:
        <i>
         dio (i)lli (el)lo
        </i>
        →
        <i>
         dio ge lo
        </i>
        →
        <i>
         diógelo
        </i>
        (arch.) →
        <i>
         dióselo
        </i>
        →
        <i>
         se lo dio
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        Portuguese:
        <i>
         deu (i)lli (l)o
        </i>
        →
        <i>
         deu lhe (l)o
        </i>
        →
        <i>
         deu-lho
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Thus, modern Spanish makes no distinction between the
       <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_pronoun" title="Reflexive pronoun">
        reflexive pronoun
       </a>
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">
        dative
       </a>
       personal pronoun
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       . Note that this did not happen in old Spanish:
       <i>
        dió
        <b>
         g
        </b>
        elo
       </i>
       , 'he gave it to him',
       <i>
        dió
        <b>
         s
        </b>
        elo
       </i>
       , 'he gave it to himself'. The medieval
       <i>
        g
       </i>
       sound (similar to that of French) was replaced with
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       in the 14th-15th centuries (cf. Spanish
       <i>
        co
        <b>
         g
        </b>
        er
       </i>
       , 'to catch', but
       <i>
        co
        <b>
         s
        </b>
        echa
       </i>
       , 'harvest', Port.
       <i>
        colher
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        colheita
       </i>
       , both from Lat.
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        colligere
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_stressed_pronouns_for_inanimate_subjects">
        Use of stressed pronouns for inanimate subjects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Use of stressed pronouns for inanimate subjects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Spanish, stressed pronouns are never used for inanimate subjects (i.e., things, as opposed to people or animals), not even for clarity or disambiguation purposes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Portuguese knows no such restriction, so that stressed pronouns referring to inanimate subjects can either be used or dropped:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         ¿Dónde están las llaves? Están en la mesa.
        </i>
        (Spanish – pronoun is often dropped)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Onde estão as chaves? (
         <b>
          Elas
         </b>
         ) estão na mesa.
        </i>
        (Portuguese – pronoun is optional)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Where are the keys? On the table.' (English – pronoun is not necessarily required)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Second-person_pronouns">
        Second-person pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Second-person pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The use of second-person pronouns differs dramatically between Spanish and Portuguese, and even more so between European and Brazilian Portuguese. Spanish
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       correspond etymologically to Portuguese
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       , but Portuguese has gained a third, even more formal form
       <i>
        o(s) senhor(es), a(s) senhora(s)
       </i>
       , demoting
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       to an "equalizing" rather than respectful register. The old familiar forms have been largely lost in the Portuguese-speaking world, as the Portuguese equalizing forms
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        vocês
       </i>
       have displaced
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       to a large extent and
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       almost entirely; and even where
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is still used, the second-person verb forms that historically corresponded to it are often replaced by the same (third-person) forms that are used with "você".
      </p>
      <p>
       In the plural, Portuguese familiar
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       is archaic nearly everywhere (as with the old English second singular "thou"), and both the subject pronoun and its corresponding second-person plural verb forms are generally limited to the Bible, traditional prayers, and spoken varieties of certain regions of rural Portugal; normally, the familiar (and equalizing) form is now
       <i>
        vocês,
       </i>
       although in Portugal the second person plural forms are retained for both object and possessive pronouns (e.g.,
       <i>
        vocês e a vossa família
       </i>
       ). In the case of Peninsular Spanish,
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        vosotros
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        ustedes
       </i>
       have more or less kept their original functions; if anything,
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       is displacing
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       out of common use and
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       is coming to be used only for formal situations (like
       <i>
        o senhor
       </i>
       in Portuguese). Latin American Spanish is more complicated:
       <i>
        vosotros
       </i>
       has fallen out of use in favor of
       <i>
        ustedes
       </i>
       , but certain regions of Spanish America also use
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       as a
       <i>
        singular
       </i>
       informal pronoun, displacing
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       out of its original role to a greater or lesser extent (see
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voseo" title="Voseo">
         voseo
        </a>
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Spoken Brazilian Portuguese has dramatically simplified the pronoun system, with
       <i>
        você(s)
       </i>
       tending to displace all other forms. Although a few parts of Brazil still use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and the corresponding second-person singular verb forms, most areas either use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       with third-person verb forms or (increasingly) drop
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       entirely in favor of
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       . This has in turn caused the original third-person possessive
       <i>
        seu, sua
       </i>
       to shift to primarily second-person use, alongside the appearance of a new third-person possessive
       <i>
        dele, dela
       </i>
       (plural
       <i>
        deles, delas
       </i>
       , "their") that follows the noun (thus paraphrases such as
       <i>
        o carro dele
       </i>
       "his car",
       <i>
        o carro dela
       </i>
       "her car"). The formal
       <i>
        o senhor
       </i>
       is also increasingly restricted to highly formal situations, such as that of a storekeeper addressing a customer, or a child or teenager addressing an adult stranger.
      </p>
      <p>
       More conservative in this regard is the
       <i>
        fluminense
       </i>
       dialect of Brazilian Portuguese (spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro_(state)" title="Rio de Janeiro (state)">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
        Espírito Santo
       </a>
       and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Zona_da_Mata_(Minas_Gerais)" title="Zona da Mata (Minas Gerais)">
        Zona da Mata
       </a>
       of the state of
       <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
        Minas Gerais
       </a>
       ) – especially its
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Carioca" title="Carioca">
         carioca
        </a>
       </i>
       sociolect. This dialect generally preserves intimate or familiar
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       , the standard equalizing form
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       , and the respectful or formal
       <i>
        o senhor
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        a senhora
       </i>
       , together with their related possessives, to such an extent that almost all speakers use these forms, according to context. Nevertheless, a minority of educated speakers correctly conjugates all of the
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       pronouns formally; otherwise, it is mostly conjugated as
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Standard Portuguese usage has
       <i>
        vocês
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        os senhores/as senhoras
       </i>
       as plurals of
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        o senhor
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        a senhora
       </i>
       , but the vernacular has also produced new forms with the second-person familiar plural function, such as
       <i>
        gente
       </i>
       (compare
       <i>
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        gente
       </i>
       as a possible colloquial variation of
       <i>
        nós
       </i>
       , "we"/"us", that should be conjugated—but commonly is not—as third-person singular),
       <i>
        pessoas
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        pessoal
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        [meu] povo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        cês
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Eye_dialect" title="Eye dialect">
        eye dialect
       </a>
       for
       <i>
        vocês
       </i>
       in colloquial pronunciation), and
       <i>
        galera
       </i>
       (the latter mainly associated with youth slang).
      </p>
      <p>
       It is often said that the
       <i>
        gaúcho
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        nordestino
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        amazofonia
       </i>
       dialects, as well as some sociolects elsewhere, such as that in and around the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Santos,_S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="Santos, São Paulo">
        Santos
       </a>
       , have preserved
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       ; but unlike in
       <i>
        fluminense
       </i>
       , the use of
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       is very limited, and entirely absent among some speakers, and
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       takes its place. In these areas, the verb with
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is conjugated in the third-person form (as with
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       ) – except among educated speakers in some urban centers such as
       <a href="/wiki/Porto_Alegre" title="Porto Alegre">
        Porto Alegre
       </a>
       and, especially,
       <a href="/wiki/Bel%C3%A9m" title="Belém">
        Belém
       </a>
       . See
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
        Brazilian Portuguese
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">
        Verbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Verbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span id=".22To_be.22">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='"To_be"'>
        "To be"
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title='Edit section: "To be"'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Spanish and Portuguese have two main
       <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">
        copulas
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        ser
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        estar
       </i>
       . For the most part, the use of these verbs is the same in both languages, but there are a few cases where it differs. The main difference between Spanish and Portuguese is in the interpretation of the concept of state versus essence and in the generalizations one way or another that are made in certain constructions. For instance,
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Está
         </b>
         prohibido fumar.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [estar]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          É
         </b>
         proibido fumar.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [ser]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Smoking
        <b>
         is
        </b>
        forbidden.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         La silla
         <b>
          está
         </b>
         hecha de madera.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [estar]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         A cadeira
         <b>
          é
         </b>
         feita de madeira.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [ser]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'The chair
        <b>
         is
        </b>
        made of wood.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Sólo uno
         <b>
          es
         </b>
         correcto.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [ser]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Só um
         <b>
          está
         </b>
         correto
        </i>
        . (Portuguese) [estar]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Only one
        <b>
         is
        </b>
        correct.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Also, the use of
       <i>
        ser
       </i>
       regarding a permanent location is much more accepted in Portuguese. Conversely,
       <i>
        estar
       </i>
       is often permanent in Spanish regarding a location, while in Portuguese, it implies being temporary or something within the immediate vicinity (same house, building, etc.)
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Nuestra oficina
         <b>
          queda
         </b>
        </i>
        (or
        <i>
         <b>
          está
         </b>
        </i>
        )
        <i>
         muy lejos.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [quedar/estar]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         O nosso escritório
         <b>
          é
         </b>
        </i>
        (or
        <i>
         <b>
          fica
         </b>
        </i>
        )
        <i>
         muito longe.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [ser/ficar]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Our office
        <b>
         is
        </b>
        very far away.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         ¿Dónde
         <b>
          está
         </b>
        </i>
        (or
        <i>
         <b>
          queda
         </b>
        </i>
        )
        <i>
         el aeropuerto?
        </i>
        (Spanish) [estar/quedar]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Onde
         <b>
          fica
         </b>
        </i>
        (or
        <i>
         <b>
          é
         </b>
        </i>
        )
        <i>
         o aeroporto?
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [ficar/ser]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Where
        <b>
         is
        </b>
        the airport?'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Because the airport is obviously not anywhere nearby,
       <i>
        ficar
       </i>
       is used in Portuguese (most common), though
       <i>
        ser
       </i>
       can also be used.
      </p>
      <p>
       Secondary copulas are
       <i>
        quedar(se)
       </i>
       in Spanish and
       <i>
        ficar
       </i>
       in Portuguese. Each can also mean 'to stay' or 'to remain.'
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Me quedé
         </b>
         dentro de la casa todo el día.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Fiquei
         </b>
         dentro de casa todo o dia.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         I stayed
        </b>
        inside the house all day.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The Spanish sentence using the reflexive form of the verb (
       <i>
        quedarse
       </i>
       ) implies that staying inside the house was voluntary, while Portuguese and English are quite ambiguous on this matter without any additional context. (See also the next section.)
      </p>
      <p>
       Both Spanish
       <i>
        quedar(se)
       </i>
       and Portuguese
       <i>
        ficar
       </i>
       can mean 'become':
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Mi abuela
         <b>
          se está quedando
         </b>
         sorda.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         A minha avó
         <b>
          está ficando
         </b>
         surda.
        </i>
        (Brazilian Portuguese and some dialects of European Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         A minha avó
         <b>
          está a ficar
         </b>
         surda.
        </i>
        (European Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'My grandmother
        <b>
         is becoming
        </b>
        deaf.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reflexive_verbs">
        Reflexive verbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Reflexive verbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_verb" title="Reflexive verb">
        Reflexive verbs
       </a>
       are somewhat more frequent in Spanish than in Portuguese, especially with actions relating to parts of the body:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Guillermo
         <b>
          se
         </b>
         quebró la pierna jugando a la pelota.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         O Guilherme quebrou a perna jogando bola.
        </i>
        (Brazilian Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         O Guilherme partiu a perna a jogar à bola.
        </i>
        (European Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'William broke his leg playing football.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span id=".22To_like.22">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='"To_like"'>
        "To like"
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title='Edit section: "To like"'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Portuguese and Spanish verbs for expressing "liking" are similar in form (
       <i>
        gostar
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        gustar
       </i>
       respectively) but different in their arrangement of
       <a href="/wiki/Argument_(linguistics)" title="Argument (linguistics)">
        arguments
       </a>
       . Arguments in linguistics are expressions that enable a verb to complete its meaning. Expressions of liking typically require two arguments: (1) a person who likes something (sometimes called the
       <a href="/wiki/Theta_role" title="Theta role">
        "experiencer"
       </a>
       ), and (2) something that the person likes (sometimes called the
       <a href="/wiki/Theta_role" title="Theta role">
        "theme"
       </a>
       ). Portuguese and Spanish (as well as English) assign different
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_cases" title="Grammatical cases">
        grammatical cases
       </a>
       to these arguments, as shown in the following table:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; width:800px; height:100px;">
       <caption>
        Argument structure with verbs of liking
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         Person who likes
        </th>
        <th>
         Thing that is liked
        </th>
        <th>
         Form
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <td>
         Subject
        </td>
        <td>
         Object of preposition
         <i>
          de
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         (Eu) gosto da música.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <td>
         Indirect object
        </td>
        <td>
         Subject
        </td>
        <td>
         Me gusta la música.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
        <td>
         Subject
        </td>
        <td>
         Direct object
        </td>
        <td>
         I like the music.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The Portuguese sentence can be translated literally as "[I] [take satisfaction] [from] [the music]", while the Spanish corresponds to "[To me] [(it) is pleasing] [the music]."
      </p>
      <p>
       It is also possible in Spanish to express it as: "(Yo) gusto de la música", although this use has become antiquated.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Auxiliary_verb_of_the_perfect">
        Auxiliary verb of the perfect
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Auxiliary verb of the perfect">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Spanish, the compound
       <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">
        perfect
       </a>
       is constructed with the
       <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
        auxiliary verb
       </a>
       <i>
        haber
       </i>
       (&lt; Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        habēre
       </span>
       ). Although Portuguese used to use its cognate verb (
       <i>
        haver
       </i>
       ) in this way, now it is more common to form these tenses with
       <i>
        ter
       </i>
       ('to have') (&lt; Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        tenēre
       </span>
       ).
       <i>
        Haver
       </i>
       is more used in Brazilian Portuguese, while
       <i>
        ter
       </i>
       is used as an auxiliary by other Iberian languages; it is much more pervasive in Portuguese. Note that most Portuguese verb tables only contain
       <i>
        ter
       </i>
       with regard to the perfect.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Yo ya
         <b>
          había
         </b>
         comido cuando mi madre volvió.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [imperfect form of
        <i>
         haber
        </i>
        ]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Eu já comera quando a minha mãe voltou.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [pluperfect inherited from
        <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
         Latin
        </a>
        ]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Eu já
         <b>
          tinha
         </b>
         comido quando a minha mãe voltou.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [imperfect form of
        <i>
         ter
        </i>
        ]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Eu já
         <b>
          havia
         </b>
         comido quando a minha mãe voltou.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [imperfect form of
        <i>
         haver
        </i>
        ]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'I
        <b>
         had
        </b>
        already eaten when my mother returned.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Imperfect_subjunctive_versus_pluperfect_indicative">
        Imperfect subjunctive versus pluperfect indicative
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Imperfect subjunctive versus pluperfect indicative">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       A class of
       <a href="/wiki/False_friend" title="False friend">
        false friends
       </a>
       between the two languages is composed of the verb forms with endings containing -
       <i>
        ra
       </i>
       -, such as
       <i>
        cantara
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        cantaras
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        cantáramos
       </i>
       , and so on. Spanish has two forms for the imperfect
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subjunctive" title="Subjunctive">
        subjunctive
       </a>
       , one with endings in
       <i>
        -se-
       </i>
       and another with endings in -
       <i>
        ra
       </i>
       - (e.g.,
       <i>
        cantase
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        cantara
       </i>
       'were I to sing'), which are usually interchangeable. In Portuguese, only
       <i>
        cantasse
       </i>
       has this value;
       <i>
        cantara
       </i>
       is employed as a
       <a href="/wiki/Pluperfect" title="Pluperfect">
        pluperfect
       </a>
       indicative, i.e., the equivalent to Spanish
       <i>
        había cantado
       </i>
       ('I had sung'). Although there is a strong tendency to use a verb phrase instead in the spoken language, like in Spanish and English (
       <i>
        tinha cantado
       </i>
       ), the simple tense is still frequent in literature.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Present_perfect">
        Present perfect
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Present perfect">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In European Spanish, as in English, the
       <a href="/wiki/Present_perfect" title="Present perfect">
        present perfect
       </a>
       is normally used to talk about an action initiated and completed in the past, which is still considered relevant or influential in the present moment. In Portuguese and Latin American Spanish, the same meaning is conveyed by the simple
       <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">
        preterite
       </a>
       , as in the examples below:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         No, gracias. Ya
         <b>
          he cenado
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Spanish, Spain) [present perfect]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         No, gracias. Ya
         <b>
          cené
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Spanish, Latin America) [preterite]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Não, obrigado. Já
         <b>
          jantei
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [preterite]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'No, thank you.
        <b>
         I have
        </b>
        already
        <b>
         dined
        </b>
        .' [present perfect]
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          He ido
         </b>
         a España dos veces.
        </i>
        (Spanish, Spain) [present perfect]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Fui
         </b>
         a España dos veces.
        </i>
        (Spanish, Latin America) [preterite]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Fui
         </b>
         a Espanha duas vezes.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [preterite]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         I have been
        </b>
        to Spain twice.' [present perfect]
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         ¿
         <b>
          Ha oído
         </b>
         usted las últimas noticias, señor?
        </i>
        (Spanish, Spain) [present perfect]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         ¿
         <b>
          Oyó
         </b>
         usted las últimas noticias, señor?
        </i>
        (Spanish, Latin America) [preterite]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         O senhor
         <b>
          ouviu
         </b>
         as últimas notícias?
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [preterite]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         Have you heard
        </b>
        the latest news, sir?' [present perfect]
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Portuguese normally uses the present perfect (
       <i>
        pretérito perfeito composto
       </i>
       ) for speaking of an event that began in the past, was repeated regularly up to the present, and could keep happening in the future. See the contrast with Spanish in the following example:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          He pensado
         </b>
         en pedirle matrimonio.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [present perfect]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         I have thought
        </b>
        of asking her/him [indirect object] to marry me [the thought has occurred to me at least once].' [present perfect]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <b>
          Tenho pensado
         </b>
         em pedi-la em casamento.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [present perfect]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        '
        <b>
         I have been thinking
        </b>
        of asking her [direct object] to marry me.' [present perfect continuous]
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       As this example suggests, the Portuguese present perfect is often closer in meaning to the English present perfect
       <i>
        continuous
       </i>
       . See also
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_verbs#Contrasting_the_preterite_and_the_perfect" title="Spanish verbs">
        Spanish verbs: Contrasting the preterite and the perfect
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_infinitive">
        Personal infinitive
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Personal infinitive">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Portuguese, uniquely among the major Romance languages, has acquired a
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_verb_conjugation" title="Portuguese verb conjugation">
        "personal infinitive"
       </a>
       , which can be used as an alternative to a subordinate clause with a finite verb in the subjunctive.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         A recepcionista pediu para
         <b>
          esperarmos
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [personal infinitive]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         A recepcionista pediu que
         <b>
          esperássemos
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [imperfect subjunctive]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         La recepcionista nos pidió que
         <b>
          esperáramos
         </b>
         /
         <b>
          esperásemos
         </b>
         .
        </i>
        (Spanish) [imperfect subjunctive]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'The receptionist asked for
        <b>
         us to wait
        </b>
        .' (literal personal infinitive translation)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'The receptionist asked that
        <b>
         we wait
        </b>
        .' (literal Portuguese imperfect subjunctive translation)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The Portuguese perfect form of the personal infinitive corresponds to one of several possible Spanish finite verbs.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Alguém nos acusou de
         <b>
          termos roubado
         </b>
         uma caneta.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Alguien nos acusó de
         <b>
          haber robado
         </b>
         un bolígrafo.
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'Somebody accused us of
        <b>
         having stolen
        </b>
        a pen.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       On some occasions, the personal infinitive can hardly be replaced by a finite clause and corresponds to a different structure in Spanish (and English):
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         O hábito de
         <b>
          fumares
         </b>
         à janela é desagradável.
        </i>
        (Portuguese, using personal infinitive. Literally, 'The habit of
        <b>
         [you] smoking
        </b>
        at the window is unpleasant.')
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         (O) teu hábito de
         <b>
          fumar
         </b>
         à janela é desagradável.
        </i>
        (Portuguese, using
        <b>
         im
        </b>
        personal infinitive. Literally, '(The) Your habit of
        <b>
         smoking
        </b>
        at the window is unpleasant.')
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Tu hábito de
         <b>
          fumar
         </b>
         junto a una ventana es desagradable.
        </i>
        (Spanish: 'Your habit of smoking close to a window is unpleasant.')
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The personal infinitive is
       <i>
        not
       </i>
       used in counterfactual situations, as these require either the future subjunctive or the imperfect subjunctive. 'If we were/had been rich...' is
       <i>
        Se fôssemos ricos...
       </i>
       , not
       <i>
        *Se sermos ricos...
       </i>
       Also, it is conjugated the same as the future subjunctive (see next section), provided the latter is not irregular (
       <i>
        ser
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        estar
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ter
       </i>
       , etc.) The personal infinitive is
       <i>
        never
       </i>
       irregular, though the circumflex accent may be dropped in writing on expanded forms (such as
       <i>
        pôr
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the first and third person singular, the personal infinitive appears no different from the unconjugated infinitive.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         É bom eu/ele
         <b>
          esperar
         </b>
         um bocadinho.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'It is good that I/he wait(s) a bit.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The above rules also apply whenever the subjects of the two clauses are the same, but independent of each other.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Para
         <b>
          chegarmos
         </b>
         cedo, temos/teremos que nos apressar.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [personal infinitive]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Para que
         <b>
          lleguemos
         </b>
         temprano, necesitamos apresurarnos.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [present subjunctive]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'For
        <b>
         us to arrive
        </b>
        early, we
        <u>
         will
        </u>
        need to hurry.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Para
         <b>
          chegarmos
         </b>
         cedo, tínhamos/teríamos que nos apressar.
        </i>
        (Portuguese) [personal infinitive]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Para que
         <b>
          llegáramos
         </b>
         /
         <b>
          llegásemos
         </b>
         temprano, necesitaríamos apresurarnos.
        </i>
        (Spanish) [imperfect subjunctive]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'For
        <b>
         us to arrive
        </b>
        early, we
        <u>
         would
        </u>
        need to hurry.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       As shown, the personal infinitive can be used at times to replace both the impersonal infinitive and the subjunctive. Spanish has no such alternative.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Future_subjunctive">
        Future subjunctive
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Future subjunctive">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The future subjunctive, now virtually obsolete in Spanish,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       continues in use in both written and spoken Portuguese. It is used in
       <a href="/wiki/Dependent_clause" title="Dependent clause">
        subordinate clauses
       </a>
       referring to a hypothetical future event or state – either
       <a href="/wiki/Adverbial_clause" title="Adverbial clause">
        adverbial clauses
       </a>
       (usually introduced by
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       'if ' or
       <i>
        quando
       </i>
       'when') or
       <a href="/wiki/Relative_clause" title="Relative clause">
        adjective clauses
       </a>
       that modify nouns referring to a hypothetical future entity. Spanish, in the analogous if-clauses, uses the present indicative
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       , and in the
       <i>
        cuando-
       </i>
       and adjective clauses uses the present subjunctive.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Se eu
         <b>
          for
         </b>
        </i>
        <i>
         eleito presidente, mudarei a lei.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Si yo
         <b>
          soy
         </b>
        </i>
        (also
        <b>
         fuere
        </b>
        <i>
         )
        </i>
        elegido presidente, cambiaré la ley.
        <i>
         (Spanish)
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        'If I
        <b>
         am
        </b>
        elected president, I will change the law.'
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Quando
         <b>
          fores
         </b>
        </i>
        <i>
         mais velho, compreenderás.
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
       <dd>
