            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/K" title="K">
             k
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/L" title="L">
             l
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/M" title="M">
             m
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/N" title="N">
             n
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/O" title="O">
             o
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/P" title="P">
             p
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/Q" title="Q">
             q
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/R" title="R">
             r
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/S" title="S">
             s
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/T" title="T">
             t
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/U" title="U">
             u
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/V" title="V">
             v
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/W" title="W">
             w
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/X" title="X">
             x
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/Y" title="Y">
             y
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/Z" title="Z">
             z
            </a>
           </i>
          </big>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Digraphs
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <big>
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Ch_(digraph)" title="Ch (digraph)">
             ch
            </a>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lh_(digraph)" title="Lh (digraph)">
             lh
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/Nh_(digraph)" title="Nh (digraph)">
             nh
            </a>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rr_(digraph)" title="Rr (digraph)">
             rr
            </a>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gu_(digraph)" title="Gu (digraph)">
             gu
            </a>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qu_(digraph)" title="Qu (digraph)">
             qu
            </a>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ss_(digraph)" title="Ss (digraph)">
             ss
            </a>
           </i>
           (
           <i>
            cc cç sc sç xc xs
           </i>
           )
          </big>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       ⟨Sc⟩ in Latin American Spanish is not called a digraph, however it is a single sound as in Brazilian Portuguese. Also Spanish has taken ⟨sh⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʃ/
       </span>
       from English as a loan sound; e.g.,
       <i>
        sherpa
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        show
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        flash
       </i>
       . Brazilian Portuguese uses the trigraph ⟨tch⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /tʃ/
       </span>
       for loanwords; e.g.,
       <i>
        tchau
       </i>
       , '
       <a href="/wiki/Ciao" title="Ciao">
        ciao
       </a>
       ',
       <i>
        tcheco
       </i>
       'Czech',
       <i>
        República Tcheca
       </i>
       'Czech Republic',
       <i>
        tchê
       </i>
       '
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Che_(Spanish)" title="Che (Spanish)">
        che
       </a>
       ' (this latter is regional), etc. European Portuguese normally replace the trigraph ⟨tch⟩ with ⟨ch⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʃ/
       </span>
       :
       <i>
        chau
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        checo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        República Checa
       </i>
       , etc.
      </p>
      <p>
       Both Spanish and Portuguese use ⟨zz⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ts/
       </span>
       (never as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /dz/
       </span>
       – this sequence appears only in loanwords from Japanese, e.g.,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azuki_bean" title="Azuki bean">
        <i>
         adzuki
        </i>
       </a>
       ) for some Italian loanwords, but in Portuguese may sometimes not be pronounced as affricate, but having an
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_phonology#Epenthesis" title="Portuguese phonology">
        epenthetic
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɨ/
       </span>
       ; e.g., Sp. and Port.
       <i>
        pizza
       </i>
       'pizza', Sp. and Port.
       <i>
        paparazzo
       </i>
       'paparazzo', etc. Spanish also utilizes ⟨tz⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ts/
       </span>
       for Basque, Catalan and Nahuatl loanwords, and ⟨tl⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /tɬ/
       </span>
       (or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /tl/
       </span>
       ) for Nahuatl loanwords; e.g.,
       <i>
        Ertzaintza
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        quetzal
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        xoloitzcuintle
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Tlaxcala
       </i>
       , etc. Portuguese utilizes ⟨ts⟩ for
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       , originarily ⟨z⟩, and Japanese loanwords.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Question_and_exclamation_marks">
        Question and exclamation marks
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Question and exclamation marks">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Only in Spanish do interrogatives and exclamations use the question mark or exclamation point respectively at the beginning of a sentence. The same punctuation marks are used, but these are inverted. This prepares the reader in advance for either a question or exclamation type of sentence.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Interrogative:
        <i>
         ¿Cuántos años tienes?
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Exclamation:
        <i>
         ¡Cuidado con el perro!
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       On the other hand, in Portuguese, a person reading aloud lengthy sentences from unfamiliar text may have to scan ahead to check if what at first appears to be a statement, is actually a question. Otherwise, it would be too late to enable proper voice inflection. Neither language has the equivalent of the
       <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
        auxiliary verb
       </a>
       <i>
        to do
       </i>
       , which is often used to begin a question in English. Both Spanish and English can place the verb before the subject noun to indicate a question, though this is uncommon in Portuguese, and almost unheard of in Brazil. In fact, most yes/no questions in Portuguese are written the same as a statement except for the final question mark.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Spanish:
        <i>
         <b>
          ¿Tiene usted
         </b>
         una medida de cuál es su exposición a estos riesgos, y
         <b>
          está usted
         </b>
         confiado de que su organización está minimizando el impacto de los mismos sobre sus accionistas, equipo de trabajo y otros grupos interesados
         <b>
          ?
         </b>
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Portuguese:
        <i>
         Você tem ideia de qual é a sua exposição a esses riscos, e tem confiança que a sua organização está minimizando os impactos resultantes, nos seus acionistas, funcionários e outros interessados
         <b>
          ?
         </b>
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        English: '
        <b>
         Do you
        </b>
        have a measure of what your exposure is to these risks, and
        <b>
         are you
        </b>
        confident that your organization is minimizing their impact on your shareholders, staff, and other interested parties
        <b>
         ?'
        </b>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Aside from changes of punctuation in written language, in speech, converting any of the above examples from a question to a statement would involve changes of both intonation and syntax in English and Spanish, but intonation only in Portuguese.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Different_spellings_for_similar_sounds">
        Different spellings for similar sounds
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Different spellings for similar sounds">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Palatal_consonant" title="Palatal consonant">
        palatal consonants
       </a>
       are spelled differently in the two languages.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th rowspan="2">
         Description
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Portuguese
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Spelling
        </th>
        <th>
         Pronunciation
        </th>
        <th>
         Spelling
        </th>
        <th>
         Pronunciation
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td style="background:#dbdbdb;">
         palatal "l"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ll
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ʎ
         </span>
         (~
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ʝ
         </span>
         )
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
          <a href="#cite_note-62">
           [62]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          lh
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ʎ
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td style="background:#dbdbdb;">
         palatal "n"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ñ
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ɲ
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          nh
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ɲ
         </span>
         (EP),
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          j̃
         </span>
         (BP)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td style="background:#dbdbdb;">
         palatal "y"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          y
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ʝ
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          i
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          j
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The symbols ⟨ll⟩ and ⟨ñ⟩ are etymological in Spanish, as the sounds they represent are often derived from Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ll
       </span>
       and
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        nn
       </span>
       (for those positions, Portuguese has simple ⟨l⟩ and ⟨n⟩; cf.
       <i>
        rodilla
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        rodela
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        peña
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        pena
       </i>
       ). The Portuguese digraphs ⟨lh⟩ and ⟨nh⟩ were adopted from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occitan" title="Occitan">
        Occitan
       </a>
       , as
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_literature" title="Occitan literature">
        poetry of the troubadours
       </a>
       was the most important influence on Portuguese literature up until the 14th century. King
       <a href="/wiki/Denis_of_Portugal" title="Denis of Portugal">
        Denis of Portugal
       </a>
       , who established Portuguese instead of Latin as the official language, was an admirer of the
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_literature" title="Occitan literature">
        poetry of the troubadours
       </a>
       and a poet himself. Examples include names such as Port.
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minho_River" title="Minho River">
         <i>
          Mi
          <b>
           nh
          </b>
          o
         </i>
        </a>
       </i>
       (Sp.
       <i>
        Mi
        <b>
         ñ
        </b>
        o
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">
         <i>
          Maga
          <b>
           lh
          </b>
          ães
         </i>
        </a>
       </i>
       (Sp.
       <i>
        Maga
        <b>
         ll
        </b>
        anes
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The letter ⟨y⟩ was used in Portuguese from the 16th to the early 20th century in
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       loans, much as in English (e.g.,
       <i>
        Psychologia
       </i>
       , modern
       <i>
        Psicologia
       </i>
       'Psychology'). The orthographic reform in 1911 officially replaced it with ⟨i⟩. The corresponding sound can be regarded as an
       <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">
        allophone
       </a>
       of the vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       in both languages. Compare Sp.
       <i>
        re
        <b>
         y
        </b>
       </i>
       ('king'),
       <i>
        ma
        <b>
         y
        </b>
        or
       </i>
       ('larger, greater, elder') with Port.
       <i>
        re
        <b>
         i
        </b>
       </i>
       ('king'),
       <i>
        ma
        <b>
         i
        </b>
        or
       </i>
       ('larger, greater').
      </p>
      <p>
       The exact pronunciation of these three consonants varies somewhat with dialect. The table indicates only the most common sound values in each language. In most Spanish dialects, the consonants written ⟨ll⟩ and ⟨y⟩ have come to be pronounced the same way, a sound merger known as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ye%C3%ADsmo" title="Yeísmo">
         yeísmo
        </a>
       </i>
       . A similar phenomenon can be found in some dialects of Brazilian Portuguese (e.g., “
       <i>
        muié
       </i>
       ” for
       <i>
        mulher
       </i>
       , 'woman'), but it is much less widespread than in Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Portuguese letter ⟨ç⟩ (
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%87" title="Ç">
        c-cedilha
       </a>
       ), based on a
       <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_script" title="Visigothic script">
        Visigothic
       </a>
       form of the letter ⟨z⟩: "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%EA%9D%A3" title="ꝣ">
        ꝣ
       </a>
       ". In Portuguese it is used before ⟨a⟩, ⟨o⟩, and ⟨u⟩ (including nasals), and never at the beginning or end of any word. It always represents the "soft
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       " sound, namely
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
         s
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       . In modern Spanish, it has been replaced by ⟨z⟩. Example:
       <i>
        cal
        <b>
         z
        </b>
        ado
       </i>
       (Sp.),
       <i>
        cal
        <b>
         ç
        </b>
        ado
       </i>
       (Port.) 'footwear'.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Correspondences_between_word_endings">
        Correspondences between word endings
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Correspondences between word endings">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Various word endings are consistently different in the two languages.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Spanish -
        <i>
         n
        </i>
        corresponds to Portuguese -
        <i>
         m
        </i>
        when in word-final position (e.g., Spanish:
        <i>
         jardín
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         algún
        </i>
        ; Portuguese:
        <i>
         jardim
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         algum
        </i>
        ). In Portuguese, word- or syllable-final ⟨m⟩ and ⟨n⟩ indicate nasalization of the previous vowel; e.g.,
        <i>
         som
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈsõ/
        </span>
        'sound' (see
        <a href="#Phonology">
         phonology
        </a>
        below). In the plural, ⟨m⟩ is replaced with an ⟨n⟩ (Spanish:
        <i>
         jardines, algunos
        </i>
        ; Portuguese:
        <i>
         jardins, alguns
        </i>
        ), that is because in these cases the ⟨m⟩ is not in word-final position anymore. Notice, some rare learned words in Portuguese and Spanish may also have a word final -
        <i>
         n
        </i>
        (e.g., Portuguese
        <i>
         abdómen/abdômen
        </i>
        'abdomen'), and -
        <i>
         m
        </i>
        (e.g., Spanish
        <i>
         tándem
        </i>
        'tandem'), respectively.
       </li>
       <li>
        Common exceptions to the above rule concern the Spanish noun endings:
        <ul>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           án
          </i>
          and -
          <i>
           ano
          </i>
          , which normally correspond to -
          <i>
           ão
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           ã
          </i>
          in Portuguese (
          <i>
           Irán
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           Irão
          </i>
          (EP)/
          <i>
           Irã
          </i>
          (BP) 'Iran',
          <i>
           hermano
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           irmão
          </i>
          'brother', and
          <i>
           huérfano
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           órfão
          </i>
          , 'orphan
          <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
           m.
          </a>
          ');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ana
          </i>
          , which corresponds to -
          <i>
           ã
          </i>
          (
          <i>
           hermana
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           irmã
          </i>
          'sister',
          <i>
           mañana
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           manhã
          </i>
          'morning',
          <i>
           huérfana
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           órfã
          </i>
          'orphan
          <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
           f.
          </a>
          ');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ón
          </i>
          / -
          <i>
           ción
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           cción
          </i>
          / -
          <i>
           sión
          </i>
          , which usually correspond to -
          <i>
           ão
          </i>
          / -
          <i>
           ção
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           (c)ção
          </i>
          /
          <i>
           -são
          </i>
          or
          <i>
           -ssão
          </i>
          (
          <i>
           melón
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           melão
          </i>
          'melon',
          <i>
           opción
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           opção
          </i>
          'option',
          <i>
           corrección
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           corre(c)ção
          </i>
          'correction',
          <i>
           pensión
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           pensão
          </i>
          'pension', or
          <i>
           admisión
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           admissão
          </i>
          'admission');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           on
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           an
          </i>
          , which corresponds to -
          <i>
           ão
          </i>
          in most monosyllables (
          <i>
           son
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           são
          </i>
          'they are',
          <i>
           tan
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           tão
          </i>
          'as, so');
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        The singular noun or adjective endings
        <i>
         -án
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         -ón
        </i>
        in Spanish both usually correspond to Portuguese
        <i>
         -ão
        </i>
        , and likewise the Spanish ending
        <i>
         -ano
        </i>
        often corresponds to Portuguese
        <i>
         -ão
        </i>
        (although there are also many Portuguese words ending in
        <i>
         -ano
        </i>
        , including
        <a href="/wiki/Demonym" title="Demonym">
         gentilics
        </a>
        such as
        <i>
         cubano
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         boliviano
        </i>
        , etc.). The
        <i>
         plurals
        </i>
        of the Portuguese words in
        <i>
         -ão
        </i>
        , however, generally preserve the historical distinctions: Portuguese -
        <i>
         ãos
        </i>
        , -
        <i>
         ães
        </i>
        , and -
        <i>
         ões
        </i>
        generally correspond to Spanish
        <i>
         -anos
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         -anes
        </i>
        , and
        <i>
         -ones
        </i>
        , respectively:
        <ul>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ãos
          </i>
          , as in
          <i>
           mão/mãos
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           mano/manos
          </i>
          , English 'hand(s)');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ães
          </i>
          , as in
          <i>
           capitão/capitães
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           capitán/capitanes
          </i>
          , English 'captain(s)');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ões
          </i>
          , as in
          <i>
           melão/melões
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           melón/melones
          </i>
          , English 'melon(s)').
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        Notable exceptions to the above rule:
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           verão/verões
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           verano(s)
          </i>
          , English 'summer(s)');
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           vulcão/vulcões
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           volcán/volcanes
          </i>
          , English 'volcano');
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           ancião
          </i>
          , which allows the three plural forms:
          <i>
           anciãos
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           anciães
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           anciões
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           anciano(s)
          </i>
          , English 'elder(s)').
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           guardião
          </i>
          , which allows the three plural forms:
          <i>
           guardiãos
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           guardiães
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           guardiões
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           guardián/guardianes
          </i>
          , English 'guardian');
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           vilão
          </i>
          , which allows the three plural forms:
          <i>
           vilãos
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           vilães
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           vilões
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           villano/villanos
          </i>
          , English 'villain');
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           João/Joões
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           Juan/Juanes
          </i>
          , English 'John'). This plural can be seen in words such as
          <i>
           joão-de-barro/joões-de-barro
          </i>
          (Red Ovenbird).
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        The 3rd person plural endings of the
        <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">
         preterite indicative tense
        </a>
        are spelled with -
        <i>
         on
        </i>
        in Spanish (
        <i>
         pensaron, vivieron
        </i>
        'they thought, they lived'), but with -
        <i>
         am
        </i>
        in Portuguese (
        <i>
         pensaram, viveram
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        In Portuguese words ending in -
        <i>
         l
        </i>
        form their plurals by dropping ⟨l⟩ and adding -
        <i>
         is
        </i>
        (-
        <i>
         eis
        </i>
        when final unstressed -
        <i>
         il
        </i>
        ):
        <i>
         caracol/caracóis
        </i>
        (Spanish
        <i>
         caracol(es)
        </i>
        , English 'snail(s)'),
        <i>
         fácil/fáceis
        </i>
        (Spanish
        <i>
         fácil(es)
        </i>
        , English 'easy').
       </li>
       <li>
        In Spanish, adjectives and nouns ending in -
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        form their plurals by replacing ⟨z⟩ with ⟨c⟩ (-
        <i>
         ces
        </i>
        ); e.g.,
        <i>
         feroz
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         feroces
        </i>
        (Portuguese
        <i>
         feroz(es)
        </i>
        , English 'ferocious'),
        <i>
         vez
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         veces
        </i>
        (Portuguese
        <i>
         vez(es)
        </i>
        English 'time(s)').
       </li>
       <li>
        Another conspicuous difference is the use of -
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        in Spanish versus -
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        in Portuguese at the end of unstressed syllables, especially when the consonant is the last letter in a word. A few examples:
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Álvarez
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         Fernández
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         Suárez
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         izquierda
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mezquino
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         lápiz
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Álvares
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         Fernandes
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         Soares
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         esquerda
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mesquinho
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         lápis
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Other correspondences between word endings are:
        <ul>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           dad(es)
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           tad(es)
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           dade
          </i>
          (s) (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           bondad(es)
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           bondade(s)
          </i>
          'goodness(es)' and
          <i>
           libertad(es)
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           liberdade(s)
          </i>
          'liberty/ies'. The word ending -
          <i>
           zade(s)
          </i>
          is also found in Portuguese, e.g.,
          <i>
           amizade(s)
          </i>
          (Spanish
          <i>
           amistad(es)
          </i>
          , English 'friendship(s)');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ud(es)
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           ude(s)
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           virtud(es)
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           virtude(s)
          </i>
          'virtue';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ble(s)
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           vel/eis
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           amable(s)
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           amável/amáveis
          </i>
          'amiable';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           je(s)
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           gem/ns
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           lenguaje(s)
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           linguagem/linguagens
          </i>
          'language(s)';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           aso
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           asso
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           escaso
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           escasso
          </i>
          'scarce';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           eso
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           esso
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           espeso
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           espesso
          </i>
          'thick';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           esa
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           essa
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           esa
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           condesa
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           condessa
          </i>
          'countess' and
          <i>
           inglesa
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           inglesa
          </i>
          'Englishwoman';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           eza
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           iça
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           eza
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           pereza
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           preguiça
          </i>
          'laziness' and
          <i>
           naturaleza
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           natureza
          </i>
          'nature';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ez
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           ice
          </i>
          and -
          <i>
           ez
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           idiotez
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           idiotice
          </i>
          'idiocy' (there are unpredictable exceptions in Portuguese, e.g.,
          <i>
           estupidez
          </i>
          'stupidity') and
          <i>
           timidez
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           timidez
          </i>
          'shyness';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           izar
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           izar
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           isar
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           realizar
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           realizar
          </i>
          'to realize/realise' and
          <i>
           analizar
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           analisar
          </i>
          'to analize/analise' (notice there are also some Spanish verbs that in -
          <i>
           isar
          </i>
          ; e.g.,
          <i>
           avisar
          </i>
          'warn',
          <i>
           pesquisar
          </i>
          'research', etc.) Brazilian Portuguese uses an alternative word ending in -
          <i>
           issar
          </i>
          in some exceptional cases; e.g.,
          <i>
           aterrissar
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           alunissar
          </i>
          (European Portuguese
          <i>
           aterrar
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           alunar
          </i>
          ; Spanish
          <i>
           aterrizar
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           alunizar
          </i>
          , English 'landing', 'moon landing');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           azar
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           açar
          </i>
          (Portuguese),
          <i>
           amenazar
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           ameaçar
          </i>
          'threaten';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           anza
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           ança
          </i>
          (Portuguese),
          <i>
           esperanza
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           esperança
          </i>
          'hope';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           encia
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           ença
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           ência
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           diferencia
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           diferença
          </i>
          'difference' and
          <i>
           ocurrencia
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           ocorrência
          </i>
          'occurrence' (in Spanish there are few exceptional words ending in -
          <i>
           enza
          </i>
          ; e.g.,
          <i>
           vergüenza
          </i>
          'shame');
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           icia
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           iça
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           ícia
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           justicia
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           justiça
          </i>
          'justice' and
          <i>
           malicia
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           malícia
          </i>
          'malice';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           izo
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           iço
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           movedizo
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           movediço
          </i>
          'moveable';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           miento
          </i>
          or -
          <i>
           mento
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           mento
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           sentimiento
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           sentimento
          </i>
          'feeling, sentiment' and
          <i>
           reglamento
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           regulamento
          </i>
          'rules, regulations';
         </li>
         <li>
          -
          <i>
           ísimo
          </i>
          (Spanish) and -
          <i>
           íssimo
          </i>
          (Portuguese), as in
          <i>
           fidelísimo
          </i>
          vs
          <i>
           fidelíssimo
          </i>
          'most loyal'.
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Accentuation_and_nasalization">
        Accentuation and nasalization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Accentuation and nasalization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Both languages use
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritics
       </a>
       to mark the
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
        stressed syllable
       </a>
       of a word whenever it is not otherwise predictable from spelling. Since Spanish does not differentiate between mid-open and mid-close vowels and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_vowels" title="Nasal vowels">
        nasal vowels
       </a>
       , it uses only one accent, the
       <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
        acute
       </a>
       . Portuguese usually uses the acute accent (
       <b>
        ´
       </b>
       ), but also uses the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumflex_accent" title="Circumflex accent">
        circumflex accent
       </a>
       (
       <b>
        ˆ
       </b>
       ) on the mid-close vowels ⟨ê⟩ and ⟨ô⟩ and the stressed (always nasal in Brasil) ⟨â⟩.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the Spanish ⟨y⟩ can be either a consonant or a vowel, as a vowel it never takes an accent. At the end of a word, the Portuguese
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthong
       </a>
       -
       <i>
        ai
       </i>
       is the equivalent of the Spanish -
       <i>
        ay
       </i>
       , however, -
       <i>
        ai
       </i>
       can have an accent on the ⟨í⟩ to
       <a href="/wiki/Hiatus_(linguistics)" title="Hiatus (linguistics)">
        break the diphthong
       </a>
       into two separate vowels, e.g.,
       <i>
        açaí
       </i>
       (three syllables). Without the accent, as in Spanish, the last syllable would be a diphthong:
       <i>
        Parag
        <u>
         uai
        </u>
       </i>
       (Portuguese) and
       <i>
        Parag
        <u>
         uay
        </u>
       </i>
       (Spanish) 'Paraguay'.
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese nasal vowels occur before ⟨n⟩ and ⟨m⟩ (see
       <a href="#Phonology">
        phonology
       </a>
       below) without an accent mark, as these consonants are not fully pronounced in such cases. The
       <a href="/wiki/Tilde" title="Tilde">
        tilde
       </a>
       (~), is only used on nasal diphthongs such as ⟨ão⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ̃w̃]
       </span>
       and ⟨õe⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [õj̃]
       </span>
       , plus the final ⟨ã⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ̃]
       </span>
       , which replaces the -
       <i>
        am
       </i>
       ending, as the latter is reserved for verbs, e.g.,
       <i>
        amanh
        <u>
         ã
        </u>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [amɐˈɲɐ̃]
       </span>
       'tomorrow'.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Initial and middle:
        <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
         vowel
        </a>
        + ⟨n⟩ +
        <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
         consonant
        </a>
        (except ⟨h⟩, ⟨p⟩ or ⟨b⟩):
        <i>
         <u>
          an
         </u>
         teced
         <u>
          en
         </u>
         te
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ger
         <u>
          in
         </u>
         g
         <u>
          on
         </u>
         ça
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         m
         <u>
          un
         </u>
         do
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <u>
          ên
         </u>
         fase
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        Initial and middle:
        <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
         vowel
        </a>
        + ⟨m⟩ +
        <a href="/wiki/Bilabial_consonant" title="Bilabial consonant">
         bilabial consonant
        </a>
        (⟨p⟩ or ⟨b⟩):
        <i>
         caç
         <u>
          am
         </u>
         ba
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <u>
          em
         </u>
         prego
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         sup
         <u>
          im
         </u>
         pa
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         p
         <u>
          om
         </u>
         ba
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         pen
         <u>
          um
         </u>
         bra
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        Final:
        <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
         vowel
        </a>
        + ⟨m⟩:
        <i>
         fizer
         <u>
          am
         </u>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <u>
          em
         </u>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ru
         <u>
          im
         </u>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         b
         <u>
          om
         </u>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         alg
         <u>
          um
         </u>
        </i>
        (except for learned words, e.g.,
        <i>
         abdóm
         <u>
          en
         </u>
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         abdôm
         <u>
          en
         </u>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         híf
         <u>
          en
         </u>
        </i>
        , etc.)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       These do not alter the rules for stress, though note endings -
       <i>
        im
       </i>
       , -
       <i>
        ins
       </i>
       and -
       <i>
        um
       </i>
       , -
       <i>
        uns
       </i>
       are stressed, as are their non-nasal counterparts (see below). A couple of two-letter words consist of only the nasal vowel:
       <i>
        em
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        um
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Phonetic vowel nasalization occurs in Spanish—vowels may get slightly nasalized in contact with nasal consonants—but it is not phonemically distinctive. In Portuguese, on the other hand, vowel nasalization is distinctive, and therefore phonemic:
       <i>
        pois
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈpojs/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈpojʃ/
       </span>
       'because' vs
       <i>
        pões
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈpõj̃s/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈpõj̃ʃ/
       </span>
       '(you) put'.
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese changes vowel sounds with (and without) accents marks. Unaccented ⟨o⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/, /o/, /ɔ/
       </span>
       ) and ⟨e⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/, /ɨ/, /e/, /ɛ/, /ɐ/
       </span>
       ), acute accented ⟨ó⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɔ/
       </span>
       ) and ⟨é⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       ), or circumflex accented ⟨ô⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o/
       </span>
       ) and ⟨e⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       ). Thus,
       <i>
        nós
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈnɔs]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈnɔʃ]
       </span>
       'we' vs
       <i>
        nos
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [nus]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [nuʃ]
       </span>
       'us',
       <i>
        avô
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [aˈvo]
       </span>
       'grandfather' vs
       <i>
        avó
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [aˈvɔ]
       </span>
       'grandmother',
       <i>
        se
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [si]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [sɨ]
       </span>
       'itself, himself, herself' reflexive pronoun vs
       <i>
        sé
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈsɛ]
       </span>
       'seat, headquarters' vs
       <i>
        sê
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈse]
       </span>
       'to be' 2nd person imperative. Spanish pronunciation makes no such distinction.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Grave_accent" title="Grave accent">
        grave accent
       </a>
       (
       <b>
        `
       </b>
       ) is also used in Portuguese to indicate the
       <a href="/wiki/Contraction_(grammar)" title="Contraction (grammar)">
        contraction
       </a>
       of the preposition
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       (to) with a few words beginning with the vowel
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       , but not to indicate stress. In other cases, it is the combination of the preposition and the feminine definite article; in other words, the equivalent of
       <i>
        a la
       </i>
       ('to the') in Spanish.
       <i>
        Às
       </i>
       is used for the plural (
       <i>
        a las
       </i>
       in Spanish).
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        (prep.) +
        <i>
         a(s)
        </i>
        (def. article 'the') =
        <i>
         à(s)
        </i>
        ('to the').
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        (prep.) +
        <i>
         aquele(s), aquela(s)
        </i>
        (pron. 'that') =
        <i>
         à
         <u>
          que
         </u>
         le(s), à
         <u>
          que
         </u>
         la(s)
        </i>
        —underlined stressed syllable—('to that').
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        (prep.) +
        <i>
         aquilo
        </i>
        (pron. n. 'that') =
        <i>
         à
         <u>
          qui
         </u>
         lo
        </i>
        ('to that').
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Diaeresis_(diacritic)" title="Diaeresis (diacritic)">
        diaeresis
       </a>
       or trema (
       <b>
        ¨
       </b>
       ) is used in Spanish to indicate ⟨u⟩ is pronounced in the sequence ⟨gu⟩; e.g.,
       <i>
        desagüe
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [deˈsaɣwe]
       </span>
       . As the Portuguese grave accent, the trema does not indicate stress. In Brazilian Portuguese it was also used for the digraphs ⟨gu⟩ and ⟨qu⟩ for the same purpose as Spanish (e.g., former BP spelling *
       <i>
        qüinqüênio
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kwĩˈkwẽɲu]
       </span>
       , EP
       <i>
        quinquénio
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kwĩˈkwɛnju]
       </span>
       'five-year period'), however since the implementation of the Portuguese Language Orthographic Agreement in Brazil, the trema was abolished (current BP spelling
       <i>
        quinquênio
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kwĩˈkwẽɲu]
       </span>
       ), and its usage was restricted to some loanwords (e.g.,
       <i>
        mülleriano
       </i>
       'Müllerian').
      </p>
      <p>
       The accentuation rules (including those of predictable stress) of Portuguese and Spanish are similar, but not identical. Discrepancies are especially pervasive in words that contain
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       in their last syllable. Note the Portuguese diphthongs
       <i>
        ei
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ou
       </i>
       are the approximate Spanish equivalent of
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       respectively, but any word ending with these diphthongs is, by default, stressed on its final syllable.
      </p>
      <p>
       Compare the following pairs of cognates, where the stress falls on the same syllable in both languages:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table cellspacing="3px">
         <tr>
          <td>
           <i>
            <u>
             ta
            </u>
            xi
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            vi
            <u>
             ví
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            bam
            <u>
             bú
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            <u>
             an
            </u>
            sia
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            <u>
             se
            </u>
            ria
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            se
            <u>
             rí
            </u>
            a
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            jar
            <u>
             dín
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            pen
            <u>
             sáis
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            pen
            <u>
             só
            </u>
           </i>
          </td>
          <td>
           (Spanish)
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           <i>
            <u>
             tá
            </u>
            xi
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            vi
            <u>
             vi
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            bam
            <u>
             bu
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            <u>
             ân
            </u>
            sia
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            <u>
             sé
            </u>
            ria
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            se
            <u>
             ri
            </u>
            a
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            jar
            <u>
             dim
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            pen
            <u>
             sais
            </u>
           </i>
           ,
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            pen
            <u>
             sou
            </u>
           </i>
          </td>
          <td>
           (Portuguese)
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Semivowel–vowel sequences are treated differently in both languages when it comes to accentuation rules. A sequence of a semivowel adjacent to a vowel is by default assumed to be read as a
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthong
       </a>
       (part of the same syllable) in Spanish, whereas it is by default assumed to be read as a
       <a href="/wiki/Hiatus_(linguistics)" title="Hiatus (linguistics)">
        hiatus
       </a>
       (belonging to different syllables) in Portuguese. For both languages, accentuation rules consistently indicate something other than the default.
      </p>
      <p>
       A consequence of this is that words that are pronounced alike in both languages are written according to different accentuation rules. Some examples:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         emer
         <u>
          gen
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Spanish),
        <i>
         emer
         <u>
          gên
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Portuguese) 'emergency'
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         tole
         <u>
          ran
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Spanish),
        <i>
         tole
         <u>
          rân
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Portuguese) 'tolerance'
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         au
         <u>
          da
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Spanish),
        <i>
         au
         <u>
          dá
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Portuguese) 'audace'
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <u>
          o
         </u>
         cio
        </i>
        (Spanish),
        <i>
         <u>
          ó
         </u>
         cio
        </i>
        (Portuguese) 'leisure'
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         con
         <u>
          ti
         </u>
         nuo
        </i>
        (Spanish),
        <i>
         con
         <u>
          tí
         </u>
         nuo
        </i>
        (Portuguese) 'continuous'
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         conti
         <u>
          nú
         </u>
         o
        </i>
        (Spanish),
        <i>
         conti
         <u>
          nu
         </u>
         o
        </i>
        (Portuguese) 'I continue'
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Another consequence (though less common) is that some words are written exactly (or almost exactly) the same in both languages, but the stress falls on different syllables:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         demo
         <u>
          cra
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Spanish,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rising_diphthong" title="Rising diphthong">
         rising diphthong
        </a>
        at the end),
        <i>
         democra
         <u>
          ci
         </u>
         a
        </i>
        (Portuguese, the stress on -
        <i>
         ci
        </i>
        -
        <a href="/wiki/Hiatus_(linguistics)" title="Hiatus (linguistics)">
         breaks the diphthong
        </a>
        ) 'democracy'
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         poli
         <u>
          cí
         </u>
         a
        </i>
        (Spanish, the stress on -
        <i>
         cí
        </i>
        - breaks the diphthong),
        <i>
         po
         <u>
          lí
         </u>
         cia
        </i>
        (Portuguese) 'police'
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Although the vocabularies of Spanish and Portuguese are similar (at times identical), the two languages differ phonologically from each other, very likely because of the stronger
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
        Celtic
       </a>
       substratum
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in Portuguese. Phonetically Portuguese bears similarities to
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       and to
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       while the phonetics of Spanish are more comparable to those of
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_language" title="Sicilian language">
        Sicilian
       </a>
       . Portuguese has a significantly larger phonemic inventory than Spanish. This may partially explain why it is generally not very intelligible to Spanish speakers despite the lexical similarity between the two languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       One of the main differences between the Spanish and Portuguese pronunciation are the vowel sounds. Standard Spanish has a basic vowel phonological system, with five phonemic vowels (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">
          a
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Mid front unrounded vowel">
          e̞
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">
          i
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Mid back rounded vowel">
          o̞
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">
          u
         </a>
         /
        </span>
       </span>
       ). Phonetic nasalization occurs in Spanish for vowels occurring between nasal consonants or when preceding a syllable-final nasal consonant (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /m/
       </span>
       ), but it is not distinctive as in Portuguese. Dialectally, there are Spanish dialects with a greater number of vowels, with some (as Murcian and Eastern Andalusian) reaching up to 8 to 10 vowel sounds. On the other hand, Portuguese has seven to nine oral vowels (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">
          a
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open central unrounded vowel">
          ɐ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        *,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">
          e
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
          ɛ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Close back unrounded vowel">
          ɨ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        *,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">
          i
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">
          o
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">
          ɔ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">
          u
         </a>
         /
        </span>
       </span>
       ) (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɐ/
       </span>
       is closer to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel#Mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">
         ə
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       in Portugal, while the
       <a href="/wiki/Close_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Close back unrounded vowel">
        near-close near-back unrounded vowel
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɨ/
       </span>
       —also rendered as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Close_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Close back unrounded vowel">
         ɯ̟
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">
         ʊ̜
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       —is only found in European Portuguese) plus five phonemic nasal vowels (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
          ɐ̃
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
          ẽ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
          ĩ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
          õ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
          ũ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
       </span>
       ) when preceding an omitted syllable-final nasal (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ⟨n⟩
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ⟨m⟩
       </span>
       ) or when is marked with a
       <a href="/wiki/Tilde" title="Tilde">
        tilde
       </a>
       (~): ⟨ã⟩ and ⟨õ⟩. This appears to be, similarly to French, a Celtic
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       phonological adaptation to Latin. Portuguese, as Catalan, uses vowel height, contrasting stressed and unstressed (reduced) vowels. Moreover, Spanish has two semivowels as allophones,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">
          j
         </a>
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_labio-velar_approximant" title="Voiced labio-velar approximant">
          w
         </a>
        </span>
        ]
       </span>
       ; while Portuguese has four, two oral
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">
          i̯
         </a>
        </span>
        ~
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">
          ɪ̯
         </a>
        </span>
        ], [
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_labio-velar_approximant" title="Voiced labio-velar approximant">
          u̯
         </a>
        </span>
        ~
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">
          ʊ̯
         </a>
        </span>
        ]
       </span>
       and two nasalized glides
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Nasal_palatal_approximant" title="Nasal palatal approximant">
          j̃
         </a>
        </span>
        ~ ɪ̯̃], [
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Nasal_labialized_velar_approximant" title="Nasal labialized velar approximant">
          w̃
         </a>
        </span>
        ~ ʊ̯̃]
       </span>
       (non-syllabic near-close vowels, as those of most English speech, are allophones of the glides in the Brazilian dialects where near-closeds are used).
      </p>
      <p>
       The following considerations are based on a comparison of standard versions of Spanish and Portuguese. Apparent divergence of the information below from anyone's personal pronunciation may indicate one's idiolect (or dialect) diverges from the mentioned standards. Information on Portuguese phonology is adapted from Celso Pedro Luft (
       <i>
        <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">
         <i>
          Novo Manual de Português
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       , 1971), and information on Spanish phonology adapted from
       <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Seco" title="Manuel Seco">
        Manuel Seco
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         <i>
          Gramática Esencial del Español
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       , 1994).
      </p>
      <p>
       Comparing the phonemic inventory of the two languages, a noticeable divergence stands out. First, standard Portuguese has more phonemes than Spanish. Also, each language has phonemes that are not shared by the other.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_phonetic_divergence">
        Early phonetic divergence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Early phonetic divergence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Spanish and Portuguese have been diverging for over a thousand years. One of the most noticeable early differences between them concerned the result of the stressed vowels of Latin:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">

         </i>

         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>

         </i>

        <td>
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">

          /aw/
         </span>

         </i>
