         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /o̞/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ou
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ow/
         </span>
         ²
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <small>
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        The vowels
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /a/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɐ/
        </span>
        occur largely in
        <a href="/wiki/Complementary_distribution" title="Complementary distribution">
         complementary distribution
        </a>
        .
        <br/>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        This diphthong has been reduced to the monophthong
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /o/
        </span>
        in many dialects of modern Portuguese.
       </small>
      </p>
      <p>
       As
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">
        vowel length
       </a>
       ceased to be distinctive in the transition from Latin to
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance
       </a>
       , the stressed vowels
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        e
       </span>
       and
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        o
       </span>
       became
       <i>
        ie
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ue
       </i>
       in Spanish whenever they were short (Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        petra
       </span>
       → Spanish
       <i>
        piedra
       </i>
       'stone'; Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        moritvr
       </span>
       → Spanish
       <i>
        muere
       </i>
       "he dies"). Similar
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongizations
       </a>
       can be found in other Romance languages (
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       <i>
        pierre
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        pietra
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       <i>
        piatră
       </i>
       ; French
       <i>
        meurt
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        muore
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        moare
       </i>
       ), but in
       <a href="/wiki/Galician-Portuguese" title="Galician-Portuguese">
        Galician-Portuguese
       </a>
       these vowels underwent a qualitative change instead (Portuguese/Galician
       <i>
        pedra
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        morre
       </i>
       ), becoming
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_height" title="Vowel height">
        lower
       </a>
       , as also happened with short
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        i
       </span>
       and short
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        u
       </span>
       in stressed syllables. The Classical Latin vowels
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /eː/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o/
       </span>
       -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /oː/
       </span>
       were correspondingly lowered in Spanish and turned into diphthongs
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /je̞/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /we̞/
       </span>
       . In Spanish, short
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        e
       </span>
       and
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        o
       </span>
       and long
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ē
       </span>
       and
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ō
       </span>
       merged into
       <a href="/wiki/Mid_vowel" title="Mid vowel">
        mid vowels
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e̞/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o̞/
       </span>
       , while in Portuguese these vowels stayed as
       <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_vowel" title="Close-mid vowel">
        close-mid
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o/
       </span>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open-mid" title="Open-mid">
        open-mid
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɔ/
       </span>
       , as in Vulgar Latin.
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese has five phonemic
       <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
        nasal vowels
       </a>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɐ̃/, /ẽ/, /ĩ/, /õ/, /ũ/
       </span>
       ), which, according to historical linguistics, arose from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">
        assimilation
       </a>
       of the nasal consonants
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /m/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       , often at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_coda" title="Syllable coda">
        end of syllables
       </a>
       . Syllable-final
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       are still written down to indicate nasalization, even though they are no longer fully pronounced, that is, either
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ⁿ]
       </span>
       (before
       <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">
        obstruents
       </a>
       ) or elided completely. In other cases, nasal vowels are marked with a
       <a href="/wiki/Tilde" title="Tilde">
        tilde
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        ã
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        õ
       </i>
       ). Not all words containing
       <i>
        vowel + n
       </i>
       have the nasal sound, as the subsequent letter must be a consonant for this to occur: e.g.,
       <i>
        anel
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɐˈnɛw/
       </span>
       ('ring') –oral/non-nasal– vs
       <i>
        anca
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈɐ̃kɐ/
       </span>
       ('hip') –nasal–.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, in some Brazilian dialects, most vowels (including the allophones present only in unstressed environment) have nasal allophones before one of the nasal consonants
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /m/, /n/, /ɲ/
       </span>
       , followed by another vowel. In other Brazilian dialects, only stressed vowels can be nasalized this way. In European Portuguese, nasalization is absent in this environment.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Portuguese
       <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
        digraph
       </a>
       <i>
        ou
       </i>
       (pronounced usually as the
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthong
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ow]
       </span>
       , but sometimes as a
       <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">
        monophthong
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o]
       </span>
       ) corresponds to the final
       <i>
        -ó
       </i>
       of Spanish
       <i>
        -ar
       </i>
       verbs in the
       <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">
        preterite
       </a>
       tense; e.g., Spanish
       <i>
        descansó
       </i>
       and Portuguese
       <i>
        descansou
       </i>
       ("he/she rested"). The Spanish irregular verb forms in
       <i>
        -oy
       </i>
       (e.g.,
       <i>
        doy
       </i>
       "I give",
       <i>
        estoy
       </i>
       "I am",
       <i>
        soy
       </i>
       "I am",
       <i>
        voy
       </i>
       "I go") correspond to Portuguese forms in
       <i>
        -ou
       </i>
       (e.g.,
       <i>
        dou
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        estou
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sou
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        vou
       </i>
       ). But in some other words, conversely, Spanish
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       corresponds to Portuguese
       <i>
        oi
       </i>
       , e.g., Spanish
       <i>
        cosa
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        coisa
       </i>
       "thing"; Spanish
       <i>
        oro
       </i>
       "gold", Portuguese usually
       <i>
        ouro
       </i>
       , but sometimes
       <i>
        oiro
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Stressed vowel alternations may occur in Portuguese, but not in Spanish:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Spanish

        </th>
       </tr>

          [ˈnwe̞βo̞]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           novo
          </i>

           <b>
            o
           </b>
           vu]
          </span>
         </p>
        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>

         </i>

          [ˈnwe̞βa]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>

          </span>
         </p>

         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>

         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           novos
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

           <b>

           </b>

          </span>
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          new (m. pl.)
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>

         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          <i>
           novas
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [ˈn
           <b>
            ɔ
           </b>
           vɐʃ]
          </span>
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          new (f. pl.)
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Unstressed_vowels">
        Unstressed vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Unstressed vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       The history of the unstressed vowels in Spanish and Portuguese is not as well known as that of the stressed vowels, but some points are generally agreed upon. Spanish has the five short vowels of classical Latin,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/, /e̞/, /i/, /o̞/, /u/
       </span>
       . It has also two semivowels,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [w]
       </span>
       , that appear in diphthongs, but these can be considered allophones of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/
       </span>
       , respectively. The pronunciation of the unstressed vowels does not differ much from that of stressed vowels. Unstressed, non-syllabic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e̞/
       </span>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o̞/
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       can be reduced to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʝ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [w̝]
       </span>
       and complete elision in some dialects; e.g.,
       <i>
        poetisa
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pw̝e̞ˈtisa]
       </span>
       ('poet'
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
        f.
       </a>
       ),
       <i>
        línea
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈlinʝa]
       </span>
       ('line'),
       <i>
        ahorita
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o̞ˈɾita]
       </span>
       ('now').
      </p>
      <p>
       The system of seven oral vowels of Vulgar Latin has been fairly well preserved in Portuguese, as in the closely related
       <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
        Galician language
       </a>
       . In Portuguese, unstressed vowels have been more unstable, both diachronically (across time) and synchronically (between dialects), producing new vowel sounds. The vowels written ⟨a⟩, ⟨e⟩ and ⟨o⟩ are pronounced in different ways according to several factors, most notably whether they are stressed, and whether they occur in the last syllable of a word. The basic paradigm is shown in the following table (it has some exceptions).
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th rowspan="2">

         </span>
        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>
        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ə/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ə/
         </span>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /a/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ə/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /e̞/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /e/
         </span>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɛ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /e ~ e̞/
         </span>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɛ ~ e̞/
         </span>
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /e ~ e̞ ~ ɛ/
         </span>
         <br/>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɪ ~ i/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɪ ~ i/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>

         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ej ~ ɐj/
         </span>

         </sup>
         )
        </td>
        <td>

          /ɨ/
         </span>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɛ/
         </span>

         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>

         </span>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɔ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /o ~ o̞/
         </span>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɔ ~ o̞/

          2
         </sup>
        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ʊ ~ u/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ʊ ~ u/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /o/
         </span>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɔ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /o/
         </span>
         <br/>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ow ~ ɐw/
         </span>
         <sup>
          3
         </sup>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /u/
         </span>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɔ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /u/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       Always nasalized in this environment in most dialects, that is,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ̃ ~ ə̃]
       </span>
      </p>
      <p>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       Mostly in Northeastern Brazil. In some other dialects (including those of northern Brazil, Rio de Janeiro, Espírito Santo, Minas Gerais and the Brazilian Federal District), this also occurs if the stressed vowel is open rather than closed (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɔ/
       </span>
       , rather than
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o/
       </span>
       ) due to
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">
        vowel harmony
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       Only in some dialects, the first mainly in the area including and surrounding Lisbon (not present in much of northern and insular Portugal, as in Brazil), and the latter mainly in some hinterland northern Portuguese accents (not present in southern and insular Portugal, as in Brazil)
      </p>
      <p>
       Brazilian unstressed vowel allophones vary according to the geographical
       <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Brazil" title="Regions of Brazil">
        region
       </a>
       of the country.
       <a href="/wiki/Near-close_vowel" title="Near-close vowel">
        Near-close
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʊ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɪ]
       </span>
       and unstressed
       <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_vowel" title="Close-mid vowel">
        close-mid
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [e]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o]
       </span>
       are found in southern and western accents, where postvocalic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       has a "soft" allophone (a flap, a coronal approximant, or a rhotic vowel), and postvocalic sibilants (written ⟨s⟩, ⟨x⟩, and ⟨z⟩) in native words are
       <i>
        always
       </i>
       alveolar
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [s, z]
       </span>
       . Meanwhile, these close allophones do not occur in the northern and eastern accents, where postvocalic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       has a "hard" allophone (velar, uvular, or glottal) and postvocalic sibilants
       <i>
        may be
       </i>
       , consistently or not, post-alveolar
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ, ʒ, ɕ, ʑ]
       </span>
       . In the accents where postvocalic sibilants are
       <i>
        always
       </i>
       post-alveolar, such as those of Florianópolis and Rio de Janeiro, or in the accents influenced by them,
       <i>
        any
       </i>
       unstressed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a ~ ɐ/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [e̞ ~ ɛ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o̞ ~ ɔ]
       </span>
       may be raised (like in Portugal), to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [u]
       </span>
       , respectively. While this is true of all colloquial BP, it is especially characteristic of the latter dialects). This increased vowel reduction is also present in accents of the
       <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Northeast Region, Brazil">
        Brazilian Northeast
       </a>
       , particularly from Alagoas to Piauí.
      </p>
      <p>
       Similar
       <a href="/wiki/Alternation_(linguistics)" title="Alternation (linguistics)">
        alternation
       </a>
       patterns to these exist in other Romance languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occitan" title="Occitan">
        Occitan
       </a>
       . Although it is mostly an allophonic variation, some dialects have developed minimal pairs that distinguish the stressed variants from the unstressed ones. The vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Close_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Close back unrounded vowel">
         ɨ
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       is often elided in connected speech (it is not present in Brazilian Portuguese).
      </p>
      <p>
       Some Brazilian dialects diphthongize stressed vowels to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ai̯], [ɛi̯], [ei̯]
       </span>
       , etc. (except
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       ), before a sibilant at the end of a syllable (written ⟨s⟩, ⟨x⟩, ⟨z⟩, or rarely, ⟨sh⟩). For instance,
       <i>
        Jesus
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʒe̞ˈzui̯s]
       </span>
       'Jesus',
       <i>
        faz
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈfai̯s]
       </span>
       'he does',
       <i>
        dez
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈdɛi̯s]
       </span>
       'ten'. This has led to the use of
       <i>
        meia
       </i>
       (meaning
       <i>
        meia dúzia
       </i>
       , 'half a dozen') for
       <i>
        seis
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [sei̯s]
       </span>
       'six' when making enumerations, to avoid any confusion with
       <i>
        três
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tɾei̯s]
       </span>
       'three' on the telephone. In
       <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">
        Lisbon
       </a>
       and surrounding areas, stressed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐj]
       </span>
       when it comes before an alveolo-palatal
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʎ/, /ɲ/, [ɕ], [ʑ]
       </span>
       or palato-alveolar
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʃ/, /ʒ/
       </span>
       consonants followed by another vowel.
      </p>
      <p>
       The orthography of Portuguese, which is partly etymological and analogical, does not indicate these sound changes. This makes the written language look deceptively similar to Spanish. For example, although
       <i>
        breve
       </i>
       ('brief') is spelled the same in both languages, it is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈbɾe̞βe̞]
       </span>
       in Spanish, but
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈbɾɛvi ~ ˈbɾɛv(ɨ)]
       </span>
       in Portuguese. In Brazilian Portuguese, in the vast majority of cases, the only difference between final -
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       and -
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       is the stress, as both are pronounced as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       . The former is unstressed, and the latter is stressed without any diacritical mark. In European Portuguese, final
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       is
       <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">
        not pronounced
       </a>
       or is pronounced as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back unrounded vowel">
         ɨ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       , unlike
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       , which is consistently
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">
        Consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Some of the most characteristic
       <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">
        sound changes
       </a>
       undergone by the consonants from Latin to Spanish and Portuguese are shown in the table below.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Latin
        </th>
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <th>
         Examples
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          cl
         </span>
         -,
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          fl
         </span>
         -,
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          pl
         </span>
         -
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ll-
         </i>
         or
         <i>
          pl
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ch-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <p>
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           clāmāre
          </span>
          → S.
          <i>
           llamar
          </i>
          , P.
          <i>
           chamar
          </i>
          <br/>
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           plumbum
          </span>
          → S.
          <i>
           plomo
          </i>
          , P.
          <i>
           chumbo
          </i>
          <br/>
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           flammam
          </span>
          → S.
          <i>
           llama
          </i>
          , P.
          <i>
           chama
          </i>
          <br/>
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
           plēnum
          </span>
          → S.
          <i>
           lleno
          </i>
          , P.
          <i>
           cheio
          </i>
          (also
          <i>
           pleno
          </i>
          )
         </p>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         <p>
          'to call'
          <br/>
          'lead' (metal)
          <br/>
          'flame'
          <br/>
          'full'
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">

          </i>

         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <small>
        *reconstructed
       </small>
      </p>
      <p>
       Peculiar to early Spanish (as in the
       <a href="/wiki/Gascon_language" title="Gascon language">
        Gascon
       </a>
       dialect of Occitan, possibly due to a
       <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">
        Basque
       </a>
       substratum) was the loss of Latin initial
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        f
       </span>
       - whenever it was followed by a vowel that did not diphthongize. Thus, Spanish
       <i>
        hijo
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        hablar
       </i>
       correspond to Portuguese
       <i>
        filho
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        falar
       </i>
       (from Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        fīlium
       </span>
       and
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        fābulāre
       </span>
       , 'son' and 'to speak' respectively). Nevertheless, Portuguese
       <i>
        fogo
       </i>
       corresponds to Spanish
       <i>
        fuego
       </i>
       (from Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        focum
       </span>
       'fire').
      </p>
      <p>
       Another typical difference concerned the result of Latin -
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        l
       </span>
       - and -
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        n
       </span>
       - in
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/intervocalic" title="wiktionary:intervocalic">
        intervocalic
       </a>
       position:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        When single, they were retained in Spanish but
        <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">
         elided
        </a>
        in Portuguese. Often, the loss of the consonant was followed by the merger of the two surrounding vowels (as in the examples in the table above), or by the insertion of an
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epenthetic_vowel" title="Epenthetic vowel">
         epenthetic vowel
        </a>
        between them (Latin
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         harēnam
        </span>
        → Spanish
        <i>
         arena
        </i>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         arẽa
        </i>
        , today
        <i>
         areia
        </i>
        'sand').
       </li>
       <li>
        When
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consonant_length" title="Consonant length">
         double
        </a>
        , they developed into the Spanish palatals ⟨ll⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Palatal lateral approximant">
          ʎ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        (merged with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_fricative#Palatal" title="Voiced palatal fricative">
          ʝ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        in most contemporary Spanish dialects) and ⟨ñ⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_nasal#Palatal_or_alveolo-palatal" title="Palatal nasal">
          ɲ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        . Indeed, the Spanish
        <a href="/wiki/%C3%91" title="Ñ">
         letter ⟨ñ⟩
        </a>
        was originally a shorthand for
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         nn
        </span>
        . In Portuguese, -
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         ll
        </span>
        - and -
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         nn
        </span>
        - just became single, ⟨l⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
          l
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        and ⟨n⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
          n
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        , respectively.
       </li>
       <li>
        When followed by the
        <a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">
         semivowel
        </a>
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         i
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         l
        </span>
        coalesced with it into a ⟨j⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">
          x
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        in Spanish. In Portuguese,
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         l
        </span>
        and
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         n
        </span>
        followed by semivowel
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         i
        </span>
        were
        <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
         palatalized
        </a>
        into ⟨lh⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Palatal lateral approximant">
          ʎ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        and ⟨nh⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_nasal#Palatal_or_alveolo-palatal" title="Palatal nasal">
          ɲ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        , respectively.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Other
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">
        consonant clusters
       </a>
       of Latin also took markedly different routes in the two languages in their archaic period:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Origin
        </th>
        <th>
         Spanish
        </th>
        <th>
         Portuguese
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
          a
          <b>
           rg
          </b>
          illam
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          a
          <b>
           rc
          </b>
          illa
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          a
          <b>
           rg
          </b>
          ila
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         'clay'
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">

         </i>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>

         </span>

         <i>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">

         <i>

          </b>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>

        </td>
        <td>
         <i>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="left">
        <td>
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">

          </b>
          āre
         </span>
         →
         <i>
          <b>
           tr
          </b>
          e
          <b>
           m'l
          </b>
          are
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <b>
           t
          </b>
          e
          <b>
           mbl
          </b>
          ar
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <b>
           tr
          </b>

          </b>

         </i>
        </td>

         'to tremble'
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Learned words such as
       <i>
        pleno, ocular, no(c)turno, tremular
       </i>
       , and so on, were not included in the examples above, since they were adapted directly from
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Classical Latin
       </a>
       in later times.
      </p>
      <p>
       The tables above represent only general trends with many exceptions, due to:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        Other phonological processes at work in old Spanish and old Portuguese, which interfered with these.
       </li>
       <li>
        Later
        <a href="/wiki/Regularization_(linguistics)" title="Regularization (linguistics)">
         regularization
        </a>
        by analogy with related words.
       </li>
       <li>
        Later borrowing of
        <a href="/wiki/Latinism" title="Latinism">
         learned words
        </a>
        directly from Latin, especially since the Renaissance, which did not respect the original sound laws.
       </li>
       <li>
        Mutual borrowing, from Spanish to Portuguese or vice versa.
       </li>
      </ol>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Synaeresis">
        Synaeresis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Synaeresis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Portuguese has tended to eliminate
       <a href="/wiki/Hiatus_(linguistics)" title="Hiatus (linguistics)">
        hiatuses
       </a>
       that were preserved in Spanish, merging similar consecutive vowels into one (often after the above-mentioned loss of intervocalic -
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        l
       </span>
       - and -
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        n
       </span>
       -). This results in many Portuguese words being one syllable shorter than their Spanish cognates:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         creído
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         leer
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mala
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         manzana
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mañana
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         poner
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         reír
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         venir
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         crido
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ler
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         má
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         maçã
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         manhã
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         pôr
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         rir
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         vir
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In other cases, Portuguese reduces consecutive vowels to a diphthong, again resulting in one syllable fewer:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         a-te-o
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         eu-ro-pe-o
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         pa-lo
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ve-lo
        </i>
        (Spanish)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         a-teu
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         eu-ro-peu
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         pau
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         véu
        </i>
        (Portuguese)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       There are nevertheless a few words where the opposite happened, such as Spanish
       <i>
        comprender
       </i>
       versus Portuguese
       <i>
        compreender
       </i>
       , from Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        comprehendere
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Different_sounds_with_the_same_spelling">
        Different sounds with the same spelling
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Different sounds with the same spelling">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Since the late Middle Ages, both languages have gone through sound shifts and mergers that set them further apart.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sibilants">
        Sibilants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Sibilants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The most marked phonetic divergence between Spanish and Portuguese in their modern period concerned the evolution of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sibilant_consonant" title="Sibilant consonant">
        sibilants
       </a>
       . In the Middle Ages, both had a rich system of seven sibilants – paired according to affrication and voicing:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
         s
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant affricate">
         ts
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative#Voiced_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">
         z
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_sibilant_affricate" title="Voiced alveolar sibilant affricate">
         dz
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">
         ʃ
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar affricate">
         tʃ
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">
         dʒ
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       (the latter probably in
       <a href="/wiki/Free_variation" title="Free variation">
        free variation
       </a>
       with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">
         ʒ
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       , as still happens today in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ladino_language" title="Ladino language">
        Ladino
       </a>
       ) – and spelled virtually the same in Spanish and Portuguese.
      </p>

      <p>
       <small>
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        Before vowels; in the coda position, there are dialectal variations within each language, not discussed here.
       </small>
       <br/>
       <small>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        Modern Portuguese has for the most part kept the medieval spelling.
       </small>
      </p>
      <p>
       After the Renaissance, the two languages reduced their inventory of sibilants, but in different ways:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Devoicing in Spanish: the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_consonant" title="Voiced consonant">
         voiced
        </a>
        sibilants written ⟨-s-⟩, ⟨z⟩ and ⟨j/g⟩ became voiceless, merging with ⟨s-/-ss-⟩, ⟨c/ç⟩ and ⟨x⟩, respectively. In many Spanish dialects, modern ⟨c/z⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
          θ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        also merged with ⟨s⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        (
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seseo" title="Seseo">
          seseo
         </a>
        </i>
        ). Later, the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar fricative">
         palato-alveolar fricative
        </a>
        ⟨x⟩
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">
          ʃ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        changed into the
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">
         velar fricative
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">
          x
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        , while ⟨ch⟩ stay unchanged
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar affricate">
          tʃ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        . Spanish spelling has been updated according to these sound changes.
       </li>
       <li>
        Deaffrication in Portuguese: the
        <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">
         affricates
        </a>
        written ⟨c/ç⟩, ⟨z⟩ and ⟨ch⟩ became plain fricatives, merging with the sibilants ⟨s-/-ss-⟩, ⟨-s-⟩ and ⟨x⟩ in most dialects, respectively. In spite of this, modern Portuguese has for the most part kept the medieval spelling.
       </li>
       <li>
        Deaffrication in Portuguese: some rural hinterland northern Portuguese dialects as well the
        <a href="/wiki/Mirandese_language" title="Mirandese language">
         Mirandese language
        </a>
        preserved the medieval distinction, still indicated by the spelling, with the former affricates being voiceless
        <a href="/wiki/Laminal_consonant" title="Laminal consonant">
         laminal
        </a>
        , voiced laminal and still voiceless post-alveolar affricate
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ/
        </span>
        , respectively, and the sibilants being voiceless
        <a href="/wiki/Apical_consonant" title="Apical consonant">
         apical
        </a>
        , voiced apical and voiceless palato-alveolar. As much of Brazilian Portuguese, these dialects have alveolar coda sibilants, though a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_apico-alveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless apico-alveolar fricative">
         voiceless apico-alveolar fricative
        </a>
        has a hushing-like sound, more similar to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʃ/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_pronunciation_differences">
        Other pronunciation differences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>

         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr align="center">
        <th rowspan="2">

         <i>

         </i>

          [b ~ β]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [b ~ β]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">

         <br/>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

        </td>

         In Spanish and European Portuguese
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>
         is
         <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
          lenited
         </a>
         after a
         <a href="/wiki/Continuant" title="Continuant">
          continuant
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>
          d
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [d ~ ð]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [d ~ ð]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
          EP
         </a>
         )
         <br/>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [d ~ dʒ]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
          BP
         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td rowspan="2" style="text-align:left;">
         In Spanish and European Portuguese
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /d/
         </span>
         is
         <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
          lenited
         </a>
         after a
         <a href="/wiki/Continuant" title="Continuant">
          continuant
         </a>
         .
         <br/>
         In all Portuguese dialects, the consonants
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /t/
         </span>
         and
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /d/
         </span>
         have
         <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">
          affricate
         </a>
         allophones, happening when before a
         <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
          palatalizing
         </a>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /i/
         </span>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">
            dʒ
           </a>
          </span>
          i ~
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal affricate">
            dʑ
           </a>
          </span>
          i]
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar affricate">
            tʃ
           </a>
          </span>
          i ~
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal affricate">
            tɕ
           </a>
          </span>
          i]
         </span>
         , mainly in Brazil), or an
         <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">
          elided
         </a>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɨ/ ~ /e̞/ ~ /ɪ/
         </span>
         or unstressed
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /i/
         </span>
         before another (that actually becomes
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɨ/
         </span>
         in Portugal), stressed one, leading to
         <a href="/wiki/Sandhi" title="Sandhi">
          sandhi
         </a>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [dVz] →
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_sibilant_affricate" title="Voiced alveolar sibilant affricate">
            dz
           </a>
           ]
          </span>
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [dVs] →
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant affricate">
            ts
           </a>
           ]
          </span>
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [tVs] → [ts]
         </span>
         , both in Brazil and Portugal).
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>
          t
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [t]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [t]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
          EP
         </a>
         )
         <br/>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [t ~ tʃ]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
          BP
         </a>
         )
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>
          g
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɡ ~ ɣ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɡ ~ ɣ]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
          EP
         </a>
         )
         <br/>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɡ]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
          BP
         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         In Spanish and European Portuguese
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɡ/
         </span>
         is
         <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
          lenited
         </a>
         after a
         <a href="/wiki/Continuant" title="Continuant">
          continuant
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         -
         <i>
          l
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [l]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɫ]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
          EP
         </a>
         )
         <br/>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [w]
         </span>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
          BP
         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         In European Portuguese syllable-final
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /l/
         </span>
         is velarized
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant#Velarized_alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
           ɫ
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         as in
         <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
          Catalan
         </a>
         (see
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_l" title="Dark l">
          dark l
         </a>
         ), while in most Brazilian dialects and some rural European ones it is vocalized to
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_labio-velar_approximant" title="Voiced labio-velar approximant">
           w
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         .
         <i>
          Caipira
         </i>
         rhoticizes to English-like ar, while
         <i>
          portunhol da pampa
         </i>
         velarizes it.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>
          r-
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          -rr-
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [r]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʁ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         In Portuguese,
         <i>
          r-
         </i>
         and
         <i>
          -rr-
         </i>
         have several possible pronunciations. In most dialects, it is a
         <a href="/wiki/Guttural_R" title="Guttural R">
          guttural
          <i>
           r
          </i>
         </a>
         as in
         <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
          French
         </a>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʀ]
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ʁ]
         </span>
         and
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [χ]
         </span>
         in Portugal and Brazil,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [x]
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɣ]
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ħ]
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [h]
         </span>
         and
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ɦ]
         </span>
         in Brazil), while in rural northern Portugal and southern Brazil it is a
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
          trilled
          <i>
           r
          </i>
         </a>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
           r
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         (like in
         <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
          Galician
         </a>
         ). In some southern and western Brazilian dialects word final
         <i>
          -r
         </i>
         may be a tap (as in Portugal and Galicia), though English-like
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_approximant" title="Alveolar approximant">
           ɹ
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_approximant" title="Retroflex approximant">
           ɻ
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         or
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/R-colored_vowel" title="R-colored vowel">
           ɚ
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         is more common, while in northern and eastern dialects it is guttural. In Spanish,
         <i>
          r-
         </i>
         and
         <i>
          -rr-
         </i>
         have kept their original pronunciation as an
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
          alveolar trill
         </a>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
           r
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         . Intervocalic
         <i>
          -r-
         </i>
         is an
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_flap" title="Alveolar flap">
          alveolar tap
         </a>
         in both languages
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_flap" title="Alveolar flap">
           ɾ
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <td>
         <i>
          v
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [b ~ β]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [v]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td align="left">
         Originally, the letters ⟨b⟩ and ⟨v⟩ stood for distinct sounds pronounced
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_stop" title="Voiced bilabial stop">
           b
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         and
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_fricative" title="Voiced bilabial fricative">
           β
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         , respectively, but the two eventually merged into a single
         <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
          phoneme
         </a>
         in Spanish. In most varieties of Portuguese they remained separate phonemes, and the
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_fricative" title="Voiced bilabial fricative">
          bilabial fricative
         </a>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [β]
         </span>
         of Old Portuguese subsequently changed into the
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiced labiodental fricative">
          labiodental fricative
         </a>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiced labiodental fricative">
           v
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
         , as in French and
         <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
          Italian
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Since no distinction is made anymore between the pronunciation of ⟨b⟩ and ⟨v⟩, Spanish spelling has been reformed according to Classical Latin. In Portuguese, the spelling of these letters is based on pronunciation, which is closer to Latin and modern Italian. This leads to some orthographic disparities:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Compare for example Spanish
        <i>
         gobierno
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         haber
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         libro
        </i>
        with Portuguese
        <i>
         governo
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         haver
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         livro
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The endings of the imperfect indicative tense of 1st. conjugation verbs (with infinitives ending in
        <i>
         -ar
        </i>
        ) are spelled with ⟨b⟩ in Spanish (
        <i>
         cantaba
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         cantabas
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         cantábamos
        </i>
        , and so on), but with ⟨v⟩ in Portuguese (
        <i>
         cantava
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         cantavas
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         cantávamos
        </i>
        , etc.)
       </li>
       <li>
        The Spanish adjectival suffix
        <i>
         -ble
        </i>
        , as in
        <i>
         posible
        </i>
        (also used in English, "possible"), corresponds to
        <i>
         -vel
        </i>
        in Portuguese:
        <i>
         possível
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In Spanish, the plosives
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        d
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        g
       </i>
       are
       <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
        lenited
       </a>
       , usually realized as "soft"
       <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
        approximants
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [β̞, ð̞, ɣ̞]
       </span>
       (here represented without the undertracks) after
       <a href="/wiki/Continuant" title="Continuant">
        continuants
       </a>
       . While similar pronunciations can be heard in
       <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
        European Portuguese
       </a>
       , most speakers of
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
        Brazilian Portuguese
       </a>
       pronounce these phonemes consistently as "hard"
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plosives" title="Plosives">
        plosives
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [b, d, ɡ]
       </span>
       . This can make a Portuguese phrase such as
       <i>
        uma bala
       </i>
       ("a bullet") sound like
       <i>
        una pala
       </i>
       ("a shovel") to a Spanish-speaker.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contact_forms">
        Contact forms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Contact forms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
         Galician language
        </a>
        shares its origin with Portuguese in
        <a href="/wiki/Galician-Portuguese" title="Galician-Portuguese">
         Galician-Portuguese
        </a>
        but has been subject to later Spanish influence.
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Castrapo" title="Castrapo">
           Castrapo
          </a>
          is a pejorative for Spanish-influenced Galician.
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Fala_language" title="Fala language">
         Fala language
        </a>
        a Galician-Portuguese language spoken in the Spanish autonomous community of Extremadura.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Barranquenho" title="Barranquenho">
         Barranquenho
        </a>
        a transitional Spanish–Portuguese dialect with Southern Spanish traits spoken in the Portuguese municipality of Barrancos.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Portu%C3%B1ol" title="Portuñol">
         Portuñol/Portunhol
        </a>
        is the name for the mixed languages spoken in the borders of Brazil with Spanish-speaking countries.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Papiamento" title="Papiamento">
         Papiamento
        </a>
        is a
        <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">
         creole language
        </a>
        with Spanish and Portuguese influences.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judaeo-Spanish_language" title="Judaeo-Spanish language">
         Judaeo-Spanish language
        </a>
        is derived from medieval Castilian language, but has been influenced by
        <a href="/wiki/Judaeo-Portuguese" title="Judaeo-Portuguese">
         Judaeo-Portuguese
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fala_d%27Ambo" title="Fala d'Ambo">
         Fala d'Ambo
        </a>
        is a creole language derived from Portuguese but influenced by the rulers of
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Guinea" title="Spanish Guinea">
         Spanish Guinea
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
